Inside Airtable’s AI Reinvention: How To Future-Proof Your Company In Rapidly Changing Times

Forbes(2.1M) ·18:02 ·1.2K views · 2026-04-21
Howie Liu (Founder and CEO, Airtable)
Airtable CEO Howie Liu argues that the "SaaS-pocalypse" is an under-calculation of the current AI disruption, necessitating a shift from traditional software to "agentic" platforms where small teams (1–10 people) can build billion-dollar revenue businesses.
TL;DR Airtable CEO Howie Liu argues that the "SaaS-pocalypse" is an under-calculation of the current AI disruption, necessitating a shift from traditional software to "agentic" platforms where small teams (1–10 people) can build billion-dollar revenue businesses. Capital...

TL;DR

Airtable CEO Howie Liu argues that the "SaaS-pocalypse" is an under-calculation of the current AI disruption, necessitating a shift from traditional software to "agentic" platforms where small teams (1–10 people) can build billion-dollar revenue businesses.

Strategic Fundraising and Financial Resilience

  • Capital Strategy: Airtable has raised $1.4 billion, maintaining over $1 billion on the balance sheet while generating $100 million+ in annual free cash flow.
  • The "Timing is Everything" Rule: Founders should only raise capital right before or during a major company inflection point. Raising during a "slog" with no clear light at the end of the tunnel is rarely successful.
  • Runway Buffers: Liu advises raising significantly more than what is immediately required.
      • Example: Airtable raised $3 million for their first round instead of $1.5 million to ensure they had a safety cushion to reach the next milestone without the pressure of a 12-month "do or die" window.

The Evolution of Airtable: From No-Code to AI-Native

  • Phase 1 (2013–2018): Focused on democratizing app creation through a pure Product-Led Growth (PLG) model with no sales team.
  • Phase 2 (2018–2023): Shifted toward Enterprise sales, targeting Fortune 500 companies with multi-million dollar contracts and high-touch leadership.
  • Phase 3 (Current): A return to its roots driven by AI. Liu believes the best AI products (like ChatGPT or Claude) succeed because they offer immediate "magic" and self-serve value, allowing companies to hit $100M in revenue faster than ever before.
  • Dual-Product Strategy:
      • Airtable Omni: An AI agent built into the core UI to help users build apps via natural language.
      • Hyperagent: A new, separate product designed to let anyone build frontier-level agents to run entire business operations.

Navigating the "SaaS-pocalypse"

  • Stacked Disruptions: Liu views AI not as a single shift (like the move from desktop to mobile), but as 3–4 stacked disruptions happening 10x faster than previous tech cycles.
  • Agentic Leverage: The future of the economy will feature "fully agentically leveraged" companies.
      • The One-Person Billion-Dollar Business: Liu agrees with Sam Altman’s prediction that extremely small teams will generate massive revenue by replacing human-heavy processes with autonomous agents.
  • Total Disruption of Work: The way companies are structured and how customers interact with services (e.g., shopping via agents rather than visiting a website like Nike.com) is fundamentally changing.

The CEO’s "Wartime" Playbook

  • Abandon "Business as Usual": In a period of total disruption, traditional management—such as 1-on-1s, all-hands meetings, and incremental efficiency—is secondary to winning the "war" of AI adaptation.
  • Staying at the "Edge": A CEO's primary job today is staying focused on the frontier of AI models (e.g., Claude 3.5/4, Opus) to understand their evolving capabilities.
  • Information Sources:
      • The X (Twitter) Ecosystem: Liu claims all groundbreaking AI news happens on X, often bypasses formal blog posts or PR cycles.
      • Real-time Awareness: Companies are launching features so fast that annual events (like Dreamforce or F8) have become obsolete as disclosure platforms.
  • Tactical Experimentation: Liu encourages leaders to get hands-on with agents immediately to develop the "vocabulary" of what is possible.
      • Action Item: Use "Hyperagent" or "Claude Code" to automate tasks that previously took days into 30-minute agent workflows.
"When apocalypse is coming—and I think 'SaaS-pocalypse' is actually underestimating the amount of disruption—you don't maintain business as usual. What matters is winning or losing the war." — Howie Liu
"I think it's going to be the default that you're going to have very small teams—maybe one to five, maybe ten people max—that build enormously leveraged companies that generate hundreds of millions, if not billions, of revenue." — Howie Liu
"If you do nothing else as a CEO except to go and stay focused on what's happening every single week... immersing in it and understanding how to translate that into your own business, you couldn't do much better than that." — Howie Liu
Howie, I want to start with talking about fundraising. Sure. Who here out here is looking to raise some money? Couple million here and there. Howie, how much has Airtable raised? We've raised 1.4 billion. Okay, 1.4 billion raised. We've kept a billion plus on the balance sheet and we now generate over 100 million per year of free cash flow so we can self-fund effectively. I think there's moments right before or right during an inflection of a company that are optimal for raising and basically you should only raise then and every other time is a horrible time to raise. Because if you're slogging it out and there's not like a, you know, kind of a light at the end of the tunnel, you're not going to be able to convince investors to come in right then. So I think timing is everything—like either at the very beginning, like before you've, you know, kind of grounded out, or like right when you're about to hit a corner or you're starting to see some inflection. Well, we we didn't always do this correctly, which is why a lot of this is, you know, great great advice in hindsight I wish I had gotten. We raised our first round basically a year and a half into working on the product. We had self-funded, it was myself and my co-founder, and you know, we had a prototype. We were going to launch imminently. So I think just always anticipating like how much are you going to need to get to the next milestone with some extra safety cushion, right? I think too many companies go out, they raise like, you know, six to 12 months of runway, they burn it all very quickly and then it's like you're giving yourself very little room for error. Gotcha. JD Vance’s Co-Founder on Space Defense, Hard Tech, and Solving Hard Problems

How I Invest w/David Weisburd(13.3K) ·36:15 ·138 views · 2026-04-21
Colin Lintz (Co-Founder, Narya Capital) David Weisburd (Host, How I Invest)
Narya co-founder Colin advocates for a "regressive" venture capital strategy that returns to the industry’s roots of solving critical national problems in defense, space, and manufacturing through concentrated, first-principles investing rather than chasing speculative market trends.
TL;DR Narya co-founder Colin advocates for a "regressive" venture capital strategy that returns to the industry’s roots of solving critical national problems in defense, space, and manufacturing through concentrated, first-principles investing rather than chasing speculative...

TL;DR

Narya co-founder Colin advocates for a "regressive" venture capital strategy that returns to the industry’s roots of solving critical national problems in defense, space, and manufacturing through concentrated, first-principles investing rather than chasing speculative market trends.

The Narya Investment Philosophy

  • "Regressive" Venture Capital: Colin argues that to be progressive in the asset class, investors should return to the original mission of venture capital: helping the government, creating real GDP growth, and improving citizens' lives through advances in science and technology.
  • The Vance Vision: Colin highlights J.D. Vance’s approach as a "first principles" investor capable of looking 5–7 years ahead.
      • Focus Areas: Advanced manufacturing, biotech, and defense in "off-the-beaten-path" geographies.
  • Concentrated Bets: The firm maintains a highly concentrated portfolio of approximately 12 companies per fund.
      • Strategy: They focus on 3–4 "big bets" where they double and triple down, relying on a mesh network of strategic LPs and engineers for technical due diligence.
  • Avoiding Mimetic Behavior: Narya intentionally avoids "busy" or trendy sectors like Crypto, NFTs, or Large Language Model (LLM) platforms. Instead, they seek out "hidden-in-plain-sight" problems that have been overlooked by the broader VC community.

Hard Tech and the "Space Economy"

  • Business Model Innovation: In "bits and atoms" businesses (hardware, pharma, energy), technical success is insufficient. Colin emphasizes that the key to venture-scale returns is finding founders who can translate complex tech into a business model with real gross margins.
  • Space as a Contested Domain: Colin views space as the next "operating system for modern power," comparable to historical air and maritime rights.
      • True Anomaly: A key portfolio company founded by ex-Space Force/Military personnel focusing on Space Domain Awareness (SDA).
      • Strategic Deterrence: The goal is protecting critical orbital assets (comms and military systems) from adversaries like China, who are advancing rapidly in offensive space capabilities.
  • Cost Catalysts: The transition from theoretical to practical space projects was driven by an order-of-magnitude reduction in launch costs, primarily led by SpaceX, allowing for the proliferation of small-sat constellations.

Operational Discipline and Scaling

  • The Danger of "War Chests": Colin warns against founders raising excessive capital to sit on the balance sheet.
      • Distraction Risk: Large cash reserves often lead to "feature creep," hiring bloat, and chasing "shiny objects" that distract from the core mission.
      • The "Lean" Advantage: Starving a balance sheet can create a necessary sense of urgency. Innovation should happen leanly to avoid the trap of sunk-cost fallacies in secondary products.
  • Product Sequencing: Founders should only move to "Product Two" when they have reached market saturation or completed the mission of "Product One." Colin advises that having senior leadership focus on two products simultaneously is where companies typically "go sideways."

Founder Archetypes and Market Trends

  • Industrial Acumen: Unlike the stereotypical 22-year-old Silicon Valley founder, Narya often backs founders in their early 30s who possess deep "insider knowledge" of industrial sectors, such as tooling or manufacturing, often gained outside of major tech hubs.
  • The Holdco Model: Colin highlights Julie Bush (ex-Palantir) and her approach to defense. Instead of chasing multi-billion-dollar "sexy" government programs, her model uses a "hub and spoke" structure to solve smaller, shorter-term government micro-projects at scale.
  • Depolarizing Through Building: Colin rejects the concept of a "parallel economy" based on ideology.
      • Ideology-Neutral Success: The best founders are often apolitical, focusing on solving energy, health, or education problems that benefit all citizens regardless of political affiliation.
"To be progressive in the asset class, you need to be regressive and return the asset class to its inception... to help our government, to help our companies create real GDP growth at scale, and to help our citizens." — Colin
"Space is this next operating system for modern power... What does it mean to be sovereign in space? How do we create systems that work with our allies? These are the complicated questions we are delving into." — Colin
"Signal to noise is not measured by how loud you are. If a founder is spending a lot of time on X or any other platform, they're not building. The best founders tend to be less active there." — Colin
So you started Narya with J.D. Vance in 2019. What was it like working with a vice president in a business context? Before we started Narya, we had been working together since 2014, really. What always made J.D. a great investor was that he was a very first-principles type investor. He was excellent at thinking about what the state of the world was today and seeing around corners as to what it might look like five to seven years from now. He would take that lens and think about how to build investment theses around that. He was also very mission-driven, I would say, and enthusiastic about themes regarding what venture dollars could do to help our country, our government specifically, and our citizens. He wanted to know how we could improve the quality of lives for most Americans with advances in science and technology, and how we could also serve founders in off-the-beaten-path geographies. When you put that all together, you had this interesting investor who was both first-principles-driven and mission-driven. When you combine that, it gets very interesting. We were able to look at all sorts of companies across a myriad of sectors including biotech, defense, and advanced manufacturing. It was really all about this idea of returning venture dollars to backing founders addressing what we see as the need to solve problems in our country. It was almost like the idea that to be progressive in the asset class, you need to be regressive and return the asset class to its inception. Venture really started out to help our government, help our companies create real GDP growth at scale, and help our citizens. Many people believe that there is a trade-off between investing in really innovative companies and getting venture-like returns with limited risk. Do you believe that there are strategies where you could invest on a risk-adjusted basis in a superior way and make some real big bets? I do. I think one of the traps—and you have to be really careful when you are thinking about some of these categories—is that as an American, you really want these things to work. You really want us to control our own destiny across all the different themes in onshoring, whether that is tied to pharmaceuticals, defense, or job creation. You do want these things to work. However, as an investor, you need to be very careful that the mission actually matches with the potential return for your cost of capital. As a venture investor, the way we think about our cost of capital is that we want to invest in companies that, if they scale and things go well along the way with probably some serendipity as well, they can return our fund on a net basis and ideally even multiples of that. As we delve into some of these categories that are, I would say, "bits and atoms" businesses where there is real hardware, there is a lot of opportunity, but business model innovation is the key. You need to find founders who, on this continuum, not only understand how to build tech and convert that into product, but then how to create a real business around it where you can get venture-style returns at scale with real gross margins. It is a subset of these businesses. Whether you think about defense, advanced manufacturing, pharmaceuticals, or energy, it is one of those areas where I am very bullish on the categories and the CAGR of these industries. But there are very few companies that actually get the tech right and translate that into business model innovation. That is sort of where we focus our energy. There is a lot of nuance to this. Can you make a lot of money in innovation? There are a couple of ways I think about it. The only truly innovative mainstream firm that I think of is Vinod Khosla. One of the things that he says—he tells this to LPs openly and he has even written about it—is that he wants to have really good returns and top-quartile returns, but he is not optimizing on returns; he is optimizing on impact. There, he is making this inherent trade-off. It is interesting, and it also self-selects a certain type of LP. Thankfully he has had great returns as well, but I think there are some rules to how to invest into very innovative companies. Unlike being a founder—if you are Elon Musk and you say you want to build a Dyson swarm around the sun—one of the advantages of doing it within the context of a company versus a fund is that now you can amalgamate talent. Now the world’s best thinkers and doers will come to you. With a VC, you don’t have that leverage. You can’t hire these 100x investment analysts that will just out-analyze everybody else. So you are kind of fixed in your constraint from a team standpoint. The main constraint that I see there is the answer to the question of can you invest in very innovative companies and make a lot of money. The answer is yes, but it must be in something that is not too bleeding edge. It needs to be frontier, but it can’t be five to ten years away because then you won’t have the follow-on capital. I think that is right. We take a lot of pride in having been ahead of the curve on a lot of themes that are now in areas that I would call very busy. Defense is a good example. There are all these founders building defense companies right now and there are a lot of very sector-focused VC funds; it is only defense or it is only advanced manufacturing. Our view on that—again back to the high CAGR in the category but being very bearish on most of the opportunities that we see—is that a lot of those companies needed to be built five years ago. That was before the problem surfaced and before our government really needed to change its procurement cycles and change how it thinks about working with tech companies to solve its issues and its ability to scale. You have this behavior where there is this mimetic behavior where people and firms tend to flock to these same ideas after they become a little bit more mainstream. Because they were historically contrarian, they think of them as contrarian ideas, but they no longer are. We try to stay away from that. But I think your point is an interesting one because we also think there are a lot of groups that say, "No, no, no, we are not mimetic; we are thinking about the future and we are going to solve for the future the way we see it evolving." It is almost impossible, and when you have investors and you don't have an evergreen fund, I think that is just a very hard investment to make. The tech risk in our minds is too high. We prefer going after these hidden-in-plain-sight problems. When everyone moves into defense, we move a little bit away from there and we go back into healthcare, which has bottomed out. A lot of investors have real scar tissue from touching healthcare, but now with advances in AI, there is stuff one can do that you couldn’t do five years ago. We try to be very nimble and curious. I think there is this barometer where you know you can’t go too far into the future, but you need to be prescient enough to be ahead of these market trends. There are a couple of ways one can do that really effectively. To your point on Khosla, you need to have aligned LPs that are really comfortable in your strategy. Part of our strategy is having a very, very concentrated portfolio: twelve companies per fund. Then you sort of double and triple down into your best companies. So it is really only like three to four big bets per fund. That gives us a lot of time to do research and to collaborate with folks in our network. We have a lot of strategic LPs that have very experienced engineers and scientists on their payroll who help us with answering some of these hard questions. You sort of have this mesh network of aligned folks that are optimistic about the future but doing it in a way that is informed, research-driven, and cynical enough that you are not going to chase stuff when everyone else is going into it. I had Roger Vincent, who came from the Cornell endowment, and he talked about this LP mistake that a lot of LPs make, which is double diversification. Sometimes you could argue triple diversification. They are diversified; they need every portfolio company to work, then they need every fund to work, and then they need the whole category to work. Some of the smartest LPs love these concentrated positions in specific funds because they know they are building a basket of funds. They are not investing only in twelve companies in the case of Narya, or twelve to fifteen; they are actually investing into the best ideas from each manager. Most of our LPs, the discussion has always been, "Okay, we are backing you for Fund 1, but we see this as a long-term bet." We understand you are trying to build a platform for the next, say, quarter-century if things continue to go well, and we can’t promise we will be with you that whole time. Is that how they are underwriting it, in that they are betting on, call it, forty companies versus just on the fund? Is that how they are getting their mind around it, in that they expect one super power-law outcome in those forty companies? It depends on the archetype of an investor. I think the larger institutions tend to think that way. Based on our performance, we have some pretty big winners in our first fund and expect to have them in our second fund as well. Hopefully that can be showcased every five years or so, but I think that is right, yes. Hopefully you don’t have to wait three funds. No, exactly. And then there is the long tail of investors, founders we have worked with, and other more individual LPs where it is a different calculus. Narya, your fund, was famously backed by Peter Thiel, Marc Andreessen, and Eric Schmidt of Google. Why would they back a VC fund? Maybe you could explain the rationale. With the Peter example, we had been working with him for a long time, so there was a good relationship there. We both thought about things in similar ways and had a good track record of investing together. I think the broader network of—you know, the reason we were able to raise a first fund, which is always really hard, but also do it with high-quality investors that actually could be really helpful to us, was that I think we were very clear about what we were trying to do. J.D. and I had been living in the Bay and we understood the behavior, but we were surprised that so many funds were going all-in on enterprise software and consumer internet. We understood the short-term financial rationale for it, but the way we looked at the opportunity, we had some cynicism about that. We wanted, going back to this idea of being mission-driven, to invest in things that are real and actually can have real impact at scale. We just saw very few funds doing that. Of course, there are some exceptions. Combining that with this comfort that there were world-class founders capable of building transformational businesses outside of the Bay, outside of California, and outside of Boston and all these hubs—because we had seen it and invested in it historically—those two things together combined with some strategic LP relationships that we had already surfaced to folks like Peter and Marc and Eric and some other well-known people, that really resonated with them. We were very clear that we weren’t going to be chasing trends. We weren’t going to do crypto, we weren’t going to do NFTs, we weren’t going to do these things that we would almost always have FOMO around. We would never invest when everyone started chasing something new that came up. Generative AI right now is another example of that. A lot of our companies I would consider AI companies, but they are not these sort of large language model platforms. Putting that all together, I think that really resonated with investors who have great pattern recognition. They said, "Okay, this is actually a unique approach." We weren't shy about saying, "Look, we're not saying consumer tech is bad, and we might do a consumer tech business." We have a great company in consumer in the religion space. But that is not the focus. It is being nimble and curious and taking a macro view to what problems are. You can't be all-in on defense; you can't be all-in on advanced manufacturing because there is only going to be one or two companies that are really special per cycle. If you miss Anduril or an equivalent, maybe defense isn't the right place to play. Maybe you should be thinking about terrestrial defense, or maybe it’s space defense. We had a lot of really interesting ideas around that, and that was how these folks got in. It also didn't compete with what they were doing. Typically, these category definers, the Andurils and the SpaceXs, they are there before the category exists. They create the category. In other words, you can’t be chasing momentum and capture these opportunities, at least not at their earliest. We get pitched a lot by founders who are trying to build the next Palantir or the next Anduril. I think perhaps the next Anduril is Anduril, right? That being said, there is always white space in these categories. We just invested in a company a couple of months ago that has a very different theory of the case regarding where there is opportunity within defense, which really resonated with us. The thesis was that you’ve got Andurils and these big companies going after the big-ticket government programs with very complex technology. These are very capital-intensive businesses where right now you need to raise billions and billions of dollars. A lot of those companies already exist that are able to win those bids. That being said, all these arms of the US government have all these shorter-term needs in what I would call almost micro-projects. They are not interesting enough to be "sexy" for some all-star founder to go after, and they are not big enough outcomes on their own where you are going to get a $10 billion-plus outcome, so venture investors tend to shy away from them. But this founder, Julie Bush, who is ex-Palantir, she said, "Well, if you do this as a holdco model—again back to turning tech to product to business model innovation—if you have business model innovation around this where you can sort of have this hub-and-spoke structure where you are sharing resources across these product companies and going after these much more micro-problems at scale, that gets really interesting." That was a very unique idea in what I would say is a crowded category. So we are not off investing in defense; it has just got to be really special. Maybe let's double-click on one of your companies, True Anomaly. How did you go about picking that company, and explain how that fits your thesis. At prior funds, I had been a somewhat prolific investor in space and defense. One of the things we were observing was that this concept of what was "theater" for defense was changing quite a bit. We had all these capabilities at a terrestrial level, and we were starting to see more companies do stuff at a maritime level. But we felt that space had evolved from this perspective area that was just going to do stuff that was very futuristic, like go to Mars or mine aquifers on the moon, like we were talking before. But then all of a sudden it got really boring with just this basic infrastructure. We saw that in between that, what was really happening was that space was going to become this next contested domain for what it means to be sovereign in space. Our most critical assets for all our communication systems and our military systems are now in orbit. How do we protect those assets? How do we understand if others are trying to impede our progress there? Through our network, we had met Even, who is the founder of True Anomaly. He had this really interesting theory of how space was going to be the next contested domain and how China was already advancing much faster than we were in developing capabilities for both offensive defense and offensive offense. We just didn’t have that. They were ex-Space Force, ex-military, ex-prime sort of DNA, putting all these interesting founders together. They sort of had this unfair advantage where they had built the playbook for how this was going to work and how the government was going to actually be able to solve these issues. But the government and the primes were too slow to actually be able to build for this next modality of potential warfare and all these tech—both hardware and software enabled—capabilities that we were going to need to have. So they said, "Well, we need to do this as a private company and work very closely with the government." They built it in the public sphere and then they went out into the private sphere. That pattern recognition for us is really important. Right as you look to where they are now, we were in at the pre-seed and they are very far along. They just won Scope and Golden Dome both for hardware and software; there is no other startup that got that. This idea of SDA, space domain awareness, is evolving into also space-based interceptors and all these other capabilities that we hopefully will never need to use, but we need to have at least from a deterrent standpoint. I think space is sort of this next operating system for modern power. Even and this team were there ahead of that. The way I like to explain it is it’s another form of air rights. If you think about recently in the war with Iran, the US and Israel now control the air rights, so they had domain over the country and could go and bomb whatever targets in Iran. Same thing now: the new air rights is space, where you could basically take lasers and reflect them off in space and then hit any target in the world. Obviously, that is the next frontier. It is a very interesting academic discussion that is now going to become quite real about what we used to have with our oceans and our skies. Now it is going up into an orbital level. What does it mean to be sovereign in space? How do we create systems around that that actually work with our allies? Who has rights to do what on the moon? There are going to be all these really complicated decisions one is going to need to make. Being able to have capabilities to protect our assets as we delve into these complicated questions and, hopefully, lowercase "b" battles, I think is going to be really important. Perhaps this is a dumb question—I’m invested in SpaceX and Varda—but I’ve never really thought about what was the catalyst for space tech as a space? Was it just SpaceX and psychologically taking away the boundary of creating space companies, or was there something technological? SpaceX is a big part of it, but there were lots of other companies that we backed historically as early as 2012. The tech allowed for costs to get down to get a payload into space in some of these ride-sharing models. Obviously, SpaceX is a big player there, but there were other companies as well. The quality of imagery technology allowed you to do Earth observation, and then all of a sudden you had this proliferation of these smallsat constellations that were able to do well. You slowly got more and more infrastructure into space. Then you could start evolving from, "Okay, we can get rockets into space and satellites work," to asking what other things we can do. Obviously, with the rise of the internet and our need for better communication and all the advances going on in the telecom space, it has required us to go up to be able to cloak the planet in better comms. Said another way, you need the cost to get into space—the launch cost—to be an order of magnitude lower to turn these theoretical projects into reality. Until it got to a certain level, it was all just theory. It was theoretically possible but not practical. It would be a government project and it would be public sector dollars to go try to do this or that. Now you’ve got private companies where it is easy to get in space, and the re-entry issues are being solved. They can go test science, they can test power, and they can test all these things in space. Then you get all these side businesses where, now that you have all this stuff in space, you have to worry about collisions and you need technology for that. Now that satellites are in orbit much longer, you need to think about repositioning them, and so you have "space tugs," which is a new subcategory. Then you need to think about refueling. You have all these businesses that layer on top of each other. But I think a lot of those businesses in of themselves are somewhat incremental and will be somewhat commoditized. This is versus what a True Anomaly is doing because they are going to have these autonomous vehicles in orbit that need advanced compute to handle all sorts of complex repositioning, maneuverability, and awareness. There is only going to be a very short list of companies that are really special doing that. That is why we are sort of bearish as investors on space tech except for a couple of companies, but very bullish on the space economy. I just finished the book on Elon that recently came out. The author wants to create a million new Elons and wants to inspire the next generation. You are in a very unique vantage point because you are investing in very frontier and deep tech at an early stage. You are seeing these founders before they are household names. What are the common traits behind these deep tech founders that truly want to change the world and want to risk their entire career on something? The really good ones are mission-driven. They see a problem and all they care about is fixing it. To be mission-driven, you don’t have to be necessarily coming in as an expert in the category like you've been building in the sector for ten years. Some of our best founders actually see something from the outside and then they come in. There are lots of different archetypes of founders that can work in that regard. This is their life's work and the passion is there, so that's the first thing. If the question is really about how you get more of them, I think you need to encourage people to have independent thought. For a subset of them, they just know this is a problem and they want to go address it. They are able to self-start and keep reinforcing that with their learning and building the toolsets they need. They just somehow know how to do it and they're training themselves, using the best technology and the best networks to go figure that out. They just won't say no. That is the biggest thing. Do most of them have some kind of financial success? Maybe we talked about acqui-hires or a couple million bucks before they go on these grand visions? Or are they just staking everything including their livelihood on this? It's a good question. Many of our founders are a little bit older because we tend to like folks who have industrial acumen. We think it's really important to have industrial acumen when you're going after these historically intractable problems. Understanding how to build a tooling and die company is not something that your average graduate of Stanford or Berkeley is going to know how to do, and probably not even care about if you're in the Bay. It is someone who has seen that living in Detroit; whether they did it or their families did it, there's sort of that insider knowledge that I think is important. Oftentimes that is from being in the industry, and so these founders aren't sixteen or twenty-two; they're maybe in their early thirties. That's a piece of it. Some of them have capital—we have some repeat founders—but many of them are bootstrapped. They're living in geographies where it's a little bit easier to live, too. They're not living in New York or San Francisco where it's really expensive. I was just listening to an interview by an early Tesla engineer. He was begging Elon to raise more cash to have on the balance sheet, and Elon was essentially starving the balance sheet to create the sense of urgency in the company. Tell me about that. Well, I've been doing this about fifteen years now. I'm not even sure I can give you an example of where a company has just sat on a ton of cash. You say, "Oh, I ask a founder why are you raising more capital?" and they say, "Oh, it's a war chest. I need to save it in case the economy shifts or in case we have these skunkwork projects and one of them really materializes." It always sounds like a good idea, but the reality is when you have the capital, it's just too hard not to use it. If it's sitting there, you’ve got to do something with it. You either hire more people or you chase more "shiny objects." You say, "Oh, okay, we're really focused on this product, but this is adjacent but not too adjacent, so we should build that, too." Then you get all this distraction. Is this coming from the board or from the founder? Who creates this pressure when you have capital to become scatterbrained, for lack of a better word? Hopefully my boards and my companies don't have too much capital. But I think a lot of it is from the bottom up, from employees saying, "Hey, we should try this too." It becomes harder to say no. What do you mean no? We have $100 million; why can't I do this? There's not one good answer for it. I think sometimes even the best founders overestimate their ability to do multiple things at once. We always talk about finding a small market you can win in and dominate. Create a healthy monopoly and a durable franchise there, and then you can expand out. There's a sequencing where you want to start ideating on some of this stuff before you're too far into this high-class problem in "monopoly land." But you need to do that leanly. Once you start putting capital into it, people don't think about sunk costs. They say, "Oh, well we're already into this for ten million; we've got to keep going," and it becomes hard to rip the cord. It's a negative flywheel that just spins faster and faster, and then you've wasted all this time. You get all this feature creep and you confuse the team. They think, "Wait, I thought we were doing that, but now we're doing this as well. Do we pivot or not?" Lean is really good. How do you know when to go into your second product? It's a really hard question to answer because it's so bespoke for each company. It depends on the senior leadership dynamic, the category they're in, what's going on at a macro level, and what we think is going to happen three years from now once that product is live. Whether I'm on the board or having these conversations directly with founders, I always say, "If you don't ever build this product and you're really successful at what your core business is, are you satisfied with the impact you will have?" The answer for our companies is almost always yes. The way I try to frame it with them is that you haven't completed that mission yet, so only start on this when you know that product two will be in market around when you've more or less completed this mission. Completing the mission doesn't mean you're done; it just means what I think of as market saturation. That is really, really hard to do. If you're on this massive uphill battle and then all of a sudden you're going downhill a little bit toward being a category-defining company, we should all be high-fiving that you're there, but you're not done yet. Just be very cautious before you get distracted. Rewards look like changing the outcome of your company by an order of magnitude. You turn a $10 billion company into a $100 billion company. I like it, but it needs to be done in a measured way with the right amount of resources. You don't want to be starved for capital and you don't want to be starved for resources. A lean team is good, and you can have a few people working on this stuff. There's a golden ratio where you don't want to worry about whether you're taking an Uber from the airport or the bus, but you don't want so much cash that you don't even think about it or you're not hyper-focused on the mission. I would argue you need the buy-in from leadership, but you don't want to have—if it's product two—designated people focusing on product two. Whether they're existing employees or you hire new people depends on the specifics. But when you start having really good people focused on product one and product two at the same time, that's where it goes sideways. Sometimes instead of fighting these impulses, if we could find ways to align with these human needs, we have more firepower to go longer and deeper. I think that's right. It's one of the things we take pride in doing; we try to really help with what I call founder blind spots. That condition you're talking about is very real. We all have experienced it and will continue to feel it. It's like, how do you stay the course but also scratch that itch to continue to be innovative? Maybe it's innovation around how you iterate on your core business. Maybe it's lowercase "i" innovation around some limited testing to see if there's real traction with a designated team that you oversee. There's lots of different ways to do it. But there's always a slightly contradictory element to anything one thinks about in venture because it's so unique and bespoke and the world is changing every day. It's really, really hard to get it right. The other danger is that you stay in your lane for too long and you're not innovative enough because you get complacent, and then the competition comes. A lot of people try to solve that through M&A. I think "buying versus building" is usually not the right answer. If you feel like you really have to buy, maybe that means you're in the wrong business anyway. Hopefully you can build something that is more innovative and you just need to be more creative. I don't like M&A sprees for sure. My best founders look at it and they say, "We went and looked; here are the six reasons why this would make sense, but the tech isn't good enough or the culture's going to get messed up." Those are the two biggest things from an M&A standpoint why you don't do it. It's also going to be distracting because you've got this whole legal process to go do it. I like your framing of it regarding these micro-changes one can make. There is doing it at the junior employee level, the board level, or the industry level. How do you become a thought leader now that you're really far along? You don't have to do M&A; just help educate the industry and help educate the government on why what you're doing is important. Turn them into a customer; they are open for business right now. People are trying to be creative at a federal level and in a bunch of states, too. There's all this stuff you can do that is net accretive to your core business. That's what we try to help with. There's a lot of polarization in this country happening, and a lot of it is driven by social media and the incentives behind it. What changes—whether structural, philosophical, or regulatory—could be changed in order to depolarize the country? I think it's ultimately a business model question. If you're incentivizing with clicks and feedback loops for information that people require, and allowing folks to best monetize off of that, you're going to have these cycles where people are just getting fed the information they want to see. They get this confirmation bias and that, at scale, turns into different camps. If your confirmation bias is one thing, you’ll go to that site, and if it's the opposite, you’ll go somewhere else. I think the systems and the infrastructure around that need to change. I'm not sure I have a great answer. We've seen a bunch of companies that claim to have an approach to it. For me, it's not an investable thesis that I'm actively pursuing. But what is really important to me, both for me personally and for what I think is a good business, is that anything further polarizing the country is not interesting to us. There's this concept of the "parallel economy." I don't really like it and I don't believe in it. I don't think it makes a good business because I think people can overestimate the importance of ideology in a business context. The best founders whom I know are not political at all. They might be political in the sense that they're pro-business or pro-capitalism, so they have some libertarian tendencies. But what they really care about—they don't care if you're red, blue, purple, or nothing. They are just busy building amazing product and hiring the best people they can to build that product. They want to come up with business model innovation around that product to create something that is useful for everyone. If everyone just focuses on that and you keep the ideology out, that's where the good businesses are being built. I think mistakes have been made historically where you try to be too aligned to a party or a specific issue. If you just really think about first principles and using tech and science to solve something that is real, that's what's going to help get us away from this. You're going to solve an energy problem that brings down the cost of living, or you're going to cure a disease that lets us live longer. You're going to change the education system so our children can figure out how to engage with AI socially in a healthy way. I don't believe that we should over-regulate it, but that's just my lane and where I can help. The best take I've heard on this is that only 1% to 3% of the population is radicalized. What you see online is actually a very, very small minority of people. If you were to do a thought experiment and walk up to somebody on the street, the odds of them being radicalized either on the right or on the left is very low. But the perception is that basically everybody's either for you or against you because of the feeds and everything. So I don't have a solution for that either, but it is good to remember that social media is not reality. It also means that politically and from a polarization standpoint, social media just incentivizes the loudest and most extreme views, but it's not actually representative of reality. Even as an investor, I always say be very cautious of loud founders. Signal-to-noise is not measured by how loud you are, and being loud is usually a bad thing. Also, if a founder's spending a lot of time on X or any other platform, they're not building. Maybe that's not a good use of time. There are times to say things and there are times not to say things, whether they're political or otherwise, but the best founders tend to be less active there. Think about what our government's trying to do right now with advances in technology on the energy side, on space policy, and on onshoring around pharma and advanced manufacturing. If you're a startup founder building in those categories, this is a super-cycle that's net accretive to what you're doing. Maybe you disagree on other hot-button issues, but you can get business done. You can collaborate in ways that are really productive not just for you and your company, but for your employees and their families. That starts creating GDP growth that lets you scale a company. I also think what happens is then you realize that we're getting stuff done together, even if we're very different on these one or two issues. Then all of a sudden you get back to this world where it's okay to be aligned not in every single way. I like to do that in business; when I'm negotiating with somebody, I'll point out the 10% where it's zero-sum. Let's talk about that and call it out. Here is the 90% where we're not; let's focus on the 90% and then we can butt heads after where we're happier. You don't have to be 100% aligned with everybody to find that area for alignment. Colin, if you could go back to the beginning of your career—you've lived this remarkable career both as an investor but also around some of the most prolific founders and investors of our time—what is one piece of advice you'd give? What would have either accelerated your career or helped you avoid costly mistakes? Peter's talked about this with his career. I think going back, I spent too much time chasing status and what in my mind appeared to be prestigious or important. But it was really stuff that, in retrospect, looked impressive on the marquee but it wasn't what I was passionate about or what I really cared about. If I were to go back, I would say to focus more on the long-term trajectory. What are the right networks you can build, and what people can you have around you who inspire you? Focus on going lockstep based on what you care about instead of what everyone else is doing. Also, this stuff takes time; let it marinate and let it compound. You don't need to have the answers all at once. If I had had that advice—I'm not sure I would have taken it at that age—but maybe I would have gotten here much faster had I just been a little more introspective on what was really important to me and stayed away from what society was saying should be important. I think about reputation as this compounding asset. It takes so many years to compound reputation. I've seen it in every walk of life and then once it's actually compounded, it's so valuable. 100%. What we do is all about reputation. It is: do founders want to work with us, and do they want to share their visions of the future with us? Reputation is not just being helpful to them and having successful outcomes; it's being open, communicative, and human with them. Having that level of compassion and genuine interest even if it's not a fit—all of that ties into reputation in a way that I think allows you to endure not just for one cycle but for a much longer period of time. Well Colin, going back to 2015 when I made my very first venture investment with Eric Anderson, my mentor, he came to me and he said, "You want to become a venture capitalist?" I'm like, "Yes." He said, "Let's put together an SPV to invest into Compass Therapeutics." I asked him what an SPV was and he explained it to me. We reached out to LPs, and you were one of those people that said yes. Thankfully the company went public. Thanks so much for supporting me throughout my career and congratulations on everything. Yeah, no, it's great to circle back. I can't believe all that time's elapsed, but you're doing a wonderful job, as is Eric. It's fun to see our friends thriving. Thank you. Thanks for watching. The economics and trends of the restaurant industry, with Tony Xu of DoorDash

Stripe(98.6K) ·87:14 ·8.2K views · 2026-04-21
Tony Xu (Cofounder and CEO, DoorDash) John Collison (Cofounder, Stripe)
DoorDash won a crowded market by prioritizing unit economics and customer retention over aggressive spending, eventually evolving from a food delivery service into a logistics platform aiming to catalog and deliver every item within a city.
TL;DR DoorDash won a crowded market by prioritizing unit economics and customer retention over aggressive spending, eventually evolving from a food delivery service into a logistics platform aiming to catalog and deliver every item within a city. Product-Led Growth: Tony Xu...

TL;DR

DoorDash won a crowded market by prioritizing unit economics and customer retention over aggressive spending, eventually evolving from a food delivery service into a logistics platform aiming to catalog and deliver every item within a city.

The DoorDash Playbook: Retention over Marketing

  • Product-Led Growth: Tony Xu attributes DoorDash’s success to focusing on a complex multivariate problem (selection, timing, quality, cost, and customer service) rather than relying on discounts.
  • Constraints as Strategy: Between 2016 and 2018, DoorDash struggled to raise capital compared to peers. This forced the team to build a product with high organic retention. Once retention was proven, they "put the pedal to the metal" on customer acquisition.
  • Extreme Customer Obsession: In 2013, with only two weeks of cash left, a system failure led to late orders. The founders chose to refund every customer—costing 40% of their remaining funds—and hand-delivered cookies at 5 a.m. to apologize.
  • The "Five Whys" of Success: Retention and frequency of use are the ultimate metrics for product differentiation. DoorDash founders performed every role—dashing, support, and sales—to understand the "last two feet" of the business.

The Harsh Economics of the Restaurant Industry

  • Thin Margins: A typical restaurant dollar breaks down into 30% food/packaging, 30% labor, and 30% rent, leaving only a 10% net profit.
  • Incremental Margin via Delivery: Takeout and delivery orders offer 3x to 5x higher incremental margins because they leverage existing rent and labor.
  • The "Regular" Problem: Unlike airlines, restaurants struggle to identify and reward "regulars" due to high staff turnover and fragmented data. DoorDash aims to democratize this via its acquisition of SevenRooms, providing restaurants with a "360-view" of guests.
  • The Hospitality-Manufacturing Spectrum: High labor costs are forcing restaurants to move toward the ends of a spectrum: either high-end "hospitality" (human connection) or "manufacturing" (delivery-only/efficiency-focused).

The Future of Autonomy: "Dot" and Drones

  • The Cataloging Challenge: Before delivery can be automated, DoorDash must solve the data problem. Most city inventory (tens of millions of items) is not cataloged or structured.
  • Introducing "Dot": DoorDash’s custom autonomous robot is designed for short, dense suburban and city routes.
      • Speed & Size: Travels up to 20 mph (1/10th the size of a car) to solve the "parking problem" that slows down human Dashers.
      • Multimodal Capability: Dot is designed to navigate roads, sidewalks, and bike lanes—the only vehicle of its kind.
      • The "Last Two Feet": True autonomy requires solving for the handoff (unloading/loading), which robotaxis (focused on passengers) do not have to solve.
  • Drones for Distance: Drones are being deployed in Australia and eventually the U.S./Europe specifically for long-distance, low-density orders where ground travel is inefficient.

Fraud and Safety in the Physical World

  • Offline Fraud: Detecting "friendly fraud" (customers claiming an order didn't arrive) is harder than online credit card fraud. DoorDash uses proprietary mapping to track the "last two feet" of a delivery to verify the exact drop-off point.
  • Trust and Safety Systems:
      • SafeChat: A tool that detects verbal altercations in-app, as 90% of physical altercations are preceded by verbal ones.
      • Real-time Emergency Response: DoorDash built an alert system that notifies all Dashers, merchants, and local law enforcement within minutes of a physical safety threat (e.g., an active shooter) in a specific vicinity.

Notable Quotes

"At the end of the day, any consumer product is judged very simply by its retention and its usage. That’s how you know whether you have a differentiated product." — Tony Xu
"Great logistics or great reliability require building systems. If you're always trying to figure it out on the fly and you're pulling some magic trick out of thin air... that is not a system, that is an exception." — Tony Xu
"Restaurants on one hand are centers of hospitality... on the other hand, technology and autonomous technology can make a difference because restaurants are almost like manufacturing production sites." — Tony Xu

TL;DR President Trump asserts that the U.S. has achieved total military victory over Iran and will maintain the Strait of Hormuz blockade until a "great deal" is signed, while simultaneously signaling a willingness to delay Fed Chair nominee Kevin Warsh's confirmation to pursue...

TL;DR

President Trump asserts that the U.S. has achieved total military victory over Iran and will maintain the Strait of Hormuz blockade until a "great deal" is signed, while simultaneously signaling a willingness to delay Fed Chair nominee Kevin Warsh's confirmation to pursue a DOJ probe into Jerome Powell’s alleged construction fraud.

Iran Conflict and Strategic Negotiations

  • Military Dominance: President Trump claims the U.S. military has "obliterated" Iran’s capabilities, specifically citing the destruction of 159 ships, their entire air force, and all anti-aircraft radar systems.
      • Casualties: He noted the loss of 13 U.S. service members during the conflict but emphasized that the Iranian regime killed 42,000 unarmed protesters in the last two months.
  • Strait of Hormuz Blockade: The President insisted the blockade will not be lifted until a final deal is reached. He stated that while Tehran has agreed to send representatives to meet with Vice President Vance, he expects to resume bombing if a signed deal is not reached by the ceasefire deadline.
  • Nuclear Strategy: Trump justified the economic and market volatility of the conflict as a necessary step to ensure Iran never acquires a nuclear weapon, which he claimed would "blow up the world."

Federal Reserve Transition and Construction Probe

  • Kevin Warsh Nomination: Trump reaffirmed his support for Kevin Warsh as the next Fed Chair, describing him as "central casting" and stating he wants him in office by June.
  • Jerome Powell "DOJ Probe": The President is refusing an "off-ramp" to drop a Department of Justice investigation into outgoing Chair Jerome Powell (whom he calls "Too Late").
      • Cost Overruns: Trump alleges a Fed building renovation that should have cost $25 million is now projected to exceed $4 billion.
      • Office Space: He joked that Warsh may need an office in the White House because the Fed building is "stripped to sheetrock" and unusable.
  • Interest Rates: Trump expressed a desire for the U.S. to have the "lowest interest rate in the world," though he acknowledged that rate hikes are effective at stopping inflation.

Trade Policy and Supreme Court Rulings

  • Tariff Refunds: A recent Supreme Court ruling (which Trump lost by two votes) requires the government to refund approximately $165 billion in tariffs.
      • Corporate Pressure: Trump specifically urged large companies like Apple and Amazon not to seek these refunds, stating, "If they don't do that, I'll remember them."
  • Judiciary Criticism: The President criticized the Supreme Court and appellate courts for a lack of "common sense" regarding tariffs, birthright citizenship, and NIL (Name, Image, Likeness) rulings that he claims have "destroyed" college sports.

Technology, Defense, and Infrastructure

  • Anthropic and AI: Trump confirmed the administration labeled AI firm Anthropic a supply chain risk because they "started telling our military how to operate" and displayed "radical left" leanings. However, he noted recent "good talks" with the company and left the door open for future Defense Department contracts.
  • Airline Mergers:
      • United-American: Trump voiced strong opposition to a rumored merger between United and American Airlines, stating he doesn't like the lack of competition.
      • Spirit Airlines: He expressed openness to a merger or federal help for Spirit Airlines to save 14,000 jobs.
  • Defense Production: The President claimed he has made it "illegal" for defense companies like Raytheon to use government funds for stock buybacks, instead forcing them to build new manufacturing plants to increase the speed of weapon delivery (e.g., Patriot systems).

Middle East Alliances

  • UAE Financial Aid: Trump confirmed the U.S. is considering a currency swap or financial lifeline for the UAE. Despite their wealth, he noted they were "hit the hardest" by Iranian missiles (1,400 incoming) and remain a key ally.
  • Saudi Arabia: He reported a positive relationship with the Saudi leadership, noting they are helping with the blockade and are pleased with the U.S.'s renewed global standing.

Well, I think it's from the standpoint that we have to find out why a small building cost close to 4 billion. It's not finished, by the way; they have a long way to go. Day One Keynote | Adobe Summit 2026 | Adobe

Adobe(336.0K) ·127:01 ·6.1K views · 2026-04-21
> "Tools don't create, people do.
TL;DR: Adobe is pivoting to the "Agential Era," launching Adobe CX Enterprise and Adobe GenStudio to empower businesses to become "Agential Enterprises" where AI agents orchestrate the entire content lifecycle—from ideation and brand-safe creation to real-time personalization...

TL;DR: Adobe is pivoting to the "Agential Era," launching Adobe CX Enterprise and Adobe GenStudio to empower businesses to become "Agential Enterprises" where AI agents orchestrate the entire content lifecycle—from ideation and brand-safe creation to real-time personalization across the "agential web."

The Shift to Agential AI and Customer Experience Orchestration

  • The Agential Era: CEO Shantanu Narayen defined this new era as a transition from feature-based tools to conversational, intuitive systems where AI agents amplify human ingenuity rather than replace it.
      • Creativity as Productivity: As communication becomes increasingly visual, Adobe is positioning creative control as a business-critical requirement for brand consistency and IP protection.
  • AI as the New User Interface: NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang highlighted that AI agents are becoming the primary interface for software, allowing users to leverage 100% of a tool's power (like Photoshop) through natural language rather than deep menu navigation.
      • The "Busy" Radiologist Paradox: Huang noted that while AI becomes "superhuman" at specific tasks, it increases overall job demand by separating the *task* from the *purpose*, citing how AI-assisted radiologists and software engineers are now more productive and in higher demand than ever.
  • Physical AI and Digital Twins: Adobe and NVIDIA are collaborating on the Omniverse to create precise 3D digital twins of products, ensuring that while AI can generate approximate backgrounds, the core brand asset remains high-fidelity and "truthful."

Introducing Adobe CX Enterprise

  • Customer Experience Orchestration: Anil Chakravarthy (President, Digital Experience) announced Adobe CX Enterprise, an end-to-end agential AI system designed to manage the customer lifecycle from prospect identification to conversion and loyalty.
  • Brand Visibility on the "Agential Web": With traffic from AI platforms to websites up 269% year-over-year, Adobe is focusing on "Generative Engine Optimization."
      • Semrush Acquisition: Adobe announced the pending acquisition of Semrush to help brands maintain visibility and accuracy within LLMs like ChatGPT, Claude, and Gemini.
  • The CX Enterprise Coworker: A specialized agent that executes multi-step tasks based on defined business goals.
      • Autonomous Actions: The coworker can identify referral traffic spikes (e.g., from a trending TV show), recommend an acquisition campaign, optimize landing pages, and generate performance reports within Microsoft Teams using "Work IQ."

Revolutionizing the Content Supply Chain with GenStudio

  • Personalization at Scale: David Wadhwani (President, Digital Media) emphasized that content demand is expected to grow 5x over the next two years, requiring a move from "manual" to "agent-accelerated" workflows.
  • Adobe Creative Agent: This new agent orchestrates across all Adobe models and tools to bring creative visions to life.
      • Velocity Gains: Internal Adobe teams reduced the time to write a creative brief from three weeks to just seven minutes using the Creative Agent.
  • Adobe Brand Intelligence: A "living, breathing" system that learns a brand's qualitative nuances—such as feedback from review cycles and annotations—to ensure every AI-generated asset is compliant and on-brand.
  • Advanced Creative Tools: New innovations in Photoshop Beta include "Rotate Object," which turns flat 2D images into 3D-manipulatable objects, and "Harmonize," which automatically adjusts tones and shadows to blend composite images.

Enterprise Implementation and the Evolving CMO Role

  • NBCUniversal’s Scale: Ashish Desai (EVP, AI & Enterprise Innovation) detailed how NBCU deployed Firefly to 2,000+ creatives. By using custom models, they successfully scaled design elements from the NBA season to the Olympics, leveraging previously approved creative intent to automate new asset generation.
  • Xfinity’s Growth Strategy: Jon Gieselman (Chief Growth Officer) discussed using Brand Intelligence as a "traffic cop" to ensure consistency across siloed teams (email, print, digital).
      • The Premium on Creativity: Gieselman argued that AI frees up time for "human insight and taste," which is increasingly valuable in a world flooded with "beige" or safe marketing.
  • Open Ecosystems: Adobe’s agential layer is built on the Model Context Protocol (MCP), ensuring interoperability with third-party agents and platforms like Microsoft Copilot and NVIDIA’s Nemo.
"Tools don't create, people do. It’s our privilege to empower all of you to create the experiences that matter to you... Creativity is the new productivity." — Shantanu Narayen, Adobe CEO
"AI is finally valuable... People love AIs that know it all, but in the final analysis, what you pay for is work done, not the fact that there’s an AI that knows it all." — Jensen Huang, NVIDIA CEO
"I find most marketing to be beige... I tell all my creative teams: walk up to what you think is the line, and then take one step across it. I’ll pull them back if they’ve gone too far." — Jon Gieselman, Xfinity CGO
Partner, as you become an agential enterprise and help you harness the power of AI to deliver personalized experiences to all of your customers. So let's begin that journey. Let's begin to tell you how we do that. To share more about Adobe's vision in the era of agential AI, I'm really excited to welcome Adobe's chair and CEO, Shantanu Narayen. Shantanu! Thank you, Anil. Hello everyone and welcome to Adobe Summit. For over 40 years, Adobe's reason for being has been to enable people to tell their stories and to create whatever is in their mind's eye. More than a billion people have used our products over that time across every visual modality, breathing life into ideas, products, brands, marketing, campaigns, experiences, and businesses. And our evolution as a company reflects the expanding role of creativity across every industry. But what's remained constant from the origins of PostScript to our latest AI innovations is our ongoing commitment to build systems that connect, empower, and inspire. Today, as we enter a new creative era with AI, our mission, empowering everyone to create, I think is more relevant than ever. It's grounded in this belief that we have that tools don't create, people do. And it's our privilege to empower all of you to create the experiences that matter to you. In this era, we're delivering on even greater value set of our customers, directly in the workflows where ideas are created, where content is produced, where documents are executed, and even more important, where these customer experiences are both orchestrated and delivered. Think about it. Every student, business professional, and small business is looking for quick and easy AI-powered applications to help them stand out. Every creator and creative professional is looking for power and precision to easily bring their creative visions to life across any media type and surface. And marketing professionals, digital practitioners, technologists, and business owners are all looking to create, deliver, and personalize content at scale across every channel in a way that inspires loyalty, it drives lifetime value, and elevates your brand at every interaction. It's this customer-focused approach that informs every facet of our AI strategy and is the North Star frankly that motivates all of our employees to invent the future with every one of you. We're living at a true inflection point, a moment where creativity and marketing are being reshaped by AI, unlocking incredible new opportunities and raising the bar for speed, for personalization, as well as for scale. And for the first time, if you think about it, AI gives us this fundamental way to overcome the fear of the blank page. New surfaces like AI chatbots and agential browsers are helping us create and consume content quicker than ever before. Conversational interfaces across these multiple media generation models that have been created further democratize creative workflows. But every channel, every device, every media type demands more creative assets, higher, faster quality, and at a greater scale than ever before. And for enterprises, these same shifts open new channels where they have to acquire and engage customers, while the new AI co-workers and agents will automate inefficient processes and deliver these successful outcomes at scale. But speed is not enough. The bar for creative differentiation and customer engagement impact has risen exponentially. And so in this crowded landscape, brands must stand out, they must connect emotionally, and build the kind of loyalty and lifetime value that will sustain their business growth. And that's why we believe at Adobe that creative control is more important than ever before. So now let's dig into how you can harness agential AI to enhance your brands, to identify and cultivate prospects, convert them to customers, engage and delight your customers, and develop their loyalty and grow your customer lifetime value. As context, specific to customer experience and marketing, there are three major trends that are shaping the industry. First, the way consumers find information and engage with your brand is changing rapidly. LLMs and AI platforms are quickly becoming a primary interface between customers and brands. ChatGPT, Claude, Gemini, Perplexity, and others are increasingly the first stop for discovery, research, advice, and comparisons. In fact, our data shows that AI traffic to US online sites increased a staggering 269% year-over-year in March. From the enterprise perspective, the pace and promise of agential AI is unprecedented. Agential architecture gives you greater flexibility than ever before to integrate applications and democratize access to business users. AI coding tools enable you to increase the productivity of your development teams dramatically. And third, in the advertising world, digital channels are predominant and budgets continue to shift towards more measurable performance-based channels like social media, connected TV, and retail and commerce media networks. Enterprises are also starting to experiment with ads in LLMs and AI platforms. Businesses that capitalize on these three major trends will have a significant and enduring competitive advantage. But to do so, you'll need to surmount some real challenges. For starters, keeping your brands front and center with customers in a world where they are turning to LLMs and personal agents first. That's a challenge for many brands. Our latest survey shows that 80% of businesses have significant gaps in how their brands show up in AI platforms. We realize you're looking to understand and looking for help on how the LLMs truly work and what you can do to make your brands and products prominent in those LLMs. Second, the proliferation of channels where you need to reach customers today across your owned channels, earned channels, and paid channels. Adobe's own marketing team, for example, delivers experiences with well over 50 channels. I'm sure you can relate to that. Measurement, attribution, calculating return on ad spend—they're all becoming significantly more complex as a result of channel proliferation. Third, the number of customer journeys that you need to manage is increasing rapidly. Every audience, all those channels, every one of your products and your sub-brands—they each demand your attention. And that's what makes the goal of true one-on-one personalization at scale, which you all want to do so that you can delight every single customer, that's what makes it a huge challenge. And of course, content will be essential and the demand for content is skyrocketing. Marketers tell us that they expect content demand to increase 5x over the next two years. And while generating content has become easier with AI, you have to ensure that every piece of content is impactful, on brand, and just the right content for each customer. So these challenges—brand visibility in AI platforms, channel proliferation, the complexity of customer journeys, demand for one-on-one personalization, and the explosion of content—they come on top of what you're already dealing with. So you have to continue everything you're doing currently and simplify that, and address the challenges of customer experience orchestration in the era of AI. To do that, you need a system that's tailored to your needs with user interfaces that combine the simplicity of conversational UIs with the power and precision of purpose-built interfaces. You need that agential reasoning layer that orchestrates seamlessly across the agents that you build, as well as third-party agents that you get from your partners. You need intelligence that turns the raw output of generative AI models into content and actions that are consistent with your business goals and your brand. And all of that has to operate in the specific context of your enterprise, based on data that you have and making sure that there's data governance and ensuring that there's no hallucination. What you need is an end-to-end agential AI-powered system that puts it all together for you so that you can deliver and optimize personalized experiences to every customer through any channel at the right moment in the customer lifecycle. CX Analytics is a comprehensive analytics system that produces timely insights across web, mobile, customer journeys, content, conversations, and LLMs. It's built on our leading Adobe Analytics and Customer Journey Analytics capabilities and provides an easy-to-use conversational interface that enables the democratization of analytics and insights across your entire organization. All of our solutions are powered by the Adobe AI Platform, which we introduced at Summit last year and where we're continuing to innovate at a rapid pace. So let's start with the agential layer. First, I am excited to introduce CX Enterprise Coworker, a specialized AI agent designed to execute tasks for you based on defined business goals. CX Enterprise Coworker enables your teams to dramatically increase their productivity while ensuring that you maintain full choice and control. CX Enterprise Coworker activates insights across our apps, helping your users where they do their work today. And it's open and flexible, architected on standards including MCP and agent-to-agent, and it can coordinate work across multiple agents by translating your business objectives into multi-step actions. For example, if your marketing team wants to increase cross-sell performance, CX Enterprise Coworker can help bring together the right agents and tools across the organization to assemble what you need for a targeted offer, like audience segments, creative assets, and performance insights. CX Enterprise Coworker can then create a plan, and once that plan is approved by your marketers, it helps execute the plan and monitors results against the goal. And then building out the agential layer, Adobe CX Enterprise makes our entire portfolio of capabilities available through agents, skills, tools, and MCPs. Adobe agents streamline time-intensive tasks and help you deliver your customer experience objectives. They are natively integrated into our products and can be managed through AEP agent orchestrator, as well as any major third-party agent orchestration system. Our Adobe Marketing Agent, integrated with Microsoft Copilot, is a great example of how we are meeting our users where they are and making our capabilities easily accessible through partner AI platforms. With Adobe CX Enterprise, we are introducing two agential systems of intelligence. First, Adobe Brand Intelligence, which brings a dynamic insights layer that's accessible by AI agents and helps ensure that every piece of content that you produce is on brand. It has a continuous learning engine for teams to rapidly create content for experiences and the content remains true to your brand identity. It'll help you move beyond static artifacts like brand guidelines, briefs, and approved assets into a system that learns constantly from qualitative and nuanced inputs such as feedback from your review cycle, annotations, rejections, and approvals. Second, Adobe Engagement Intelligence, which is a decisioning engine optimized for customer lifetime value to drive personalization at scale. This decisioning engine enables goal-based continuous optimization based on comprehensive measurement and analytics. Adobe CX Enterprise is underpinned by our global real-time Adobe Experience Platform.
