And so I end up at Merrill Lynch and had some amazing mentors that were really inspiring to me. I had two major stops at Merrill, one on the capital markets side and then after doing that for a while I wanted to go see how these crazy securities, derivative securities, traded. So I had a chance to move to the Merrill Lynch derivatives desk. I think what was just so fun for me about reflecting back on that time is I actually never picked biotech. When I got moved over to the derivatives desk, I was assigned to the biotech subgroup of the derivatives desk and knew nothing about it but I fell in love with it so quickly because the risk-reward in that setting was about life and death. When I was looking at what was going on in the companies that I needed to be understanding deeply enough to price risk, it was like wild to me. I had no idea about this entire field of the development of medicine. I had no real appreciation for just the sheer capital that's required to move it forward. And here I am through total serendipity, I find myself at the epicenter of needing to understand these companies deeply enough to price risk. I was getting a chance to be in front of all of the buy-side investors that are building and backing these companies. I was so inspired by it that in my seat I got to be out with these people a lot because they were curious what activity was going on on the trading desk and what it meant for them and their investments. I was always interested in learning more about how they build and back these companies. What I came to quickly realize in talking to all these people was that their strategies were actually very similar. So I said, "Huh, knowing what I know about derivative securities and that I had learned from my days at MIT and my time at Merrill Lynch, and recognizing that most people in the life science investment world were just using equity securities, it struck me that there was a better way to build these portfolios and select securities." There were ways in which we might be able to either maintain the type of return that they needed for their investors but at lower risk, or maintain the risk and produce a higher rate of return. And so I went off and I spent my nights and weekends writing this beautiful deck that I thought had really cracked the code on what portfolios needed to look like in the life sciences sector. It would allow a scale of capital to get behind these amazing medicines that had never before been achieved. When it was done I could not wait to send it out. I remember telling my roommate Dom, I was like, "Dom I'm ready to send this out, I'm going to send it to everybody and you just watch, my phone is going to be ringing off the hook, everybody in the world is going to want to do this." I told him: "My days at Merrill Lynch are numbered, I'm going to be out of here soon enough." I sent it out, crickets. I didn't get one single response. And I couldn't believe it because I'm like, how does nobody want to do this? How does nobody want to run a portfolio that can produce the same amount of reward but with lower risk? It didn't make any sense to me. It turns out that months went by before my phone finally rang, but then one day it did and it was—who today is a dear friend of mine and mentor—Jeff Kaplan, who was the head of trading at, at that time in 2005, a tiny firm named Deerfield Management in its early days where Jim Flynn had just taken over as general partner. Jim was a really unique leader of Deerfield because one of the things that I learned from spending time with him was he obsessed over what is every which way by which our investment firm can have every type of advantage in producing our returns so that we can attract more and more capital and have more and more impact on the future of human health. How do we create advantages that would allow Deerfield to become the epicenter of capital to move innovation? I got lucky; I found like the one person in the world who cared about the things that I had to say. After going and presenting on my ideas there for what at that point was a tiny team of about 12 people, I got hired to join Deerfield. I spent then the next 15 years of my career helping to build Deerfield into that dream state of being the go-to source of capital for the industry. I didn't realize it at the time but I guess I was 24 when I joined and I think the next youngest person at the firm was probably 40. These were all people that were super seasoned and had incredible experience. I think I was a bit of a novelty in the room because I would ask like almost a thousand questions every single day and these amazing people would spend the time to teach me. And so the apprenticeship that I was able to get from my time at Deerfield was like nothing I could have ever imagined and I was all in. I was fully in love with investing in building and backing anything—whether it was a medical device, a diagnostic, a medicine—anything that could move human health forward. That spanned everything from helping these companies figure out how to finance their innovation all the way through to spending time with our team on how we design and help a company design a clinical study. We wanted to design it in a way that would give them an advantage to showing something that's really special. That stayed true for about the first decade of my time at Deerfield. And then something just really, really tragic happened. At Deerfield, we were constantly building capabilities and constantly wanting new sources of data. One of the sources of data that we had accumulated and had built out was now taking us deeper and deeper into understanding a patient's journey from the moment where they are prescribed a medicine to where they actually get the insurance approval to get that medicine and then how long they stay on the medicine. And what I was quickly learning was that yes, medicine only works if people take it, but they weren't. That was a really dark period for me in my own journey because as I was reflecting on all the time and effort and capital that I was a part of putting behind innovation, it became pretty quickly evident to me that you have to start asking the questions: well, for what purpose? If people need to be on a medicine for the rest of their lives and they're taking it for a year on average and then quitting, are we just optimizing for maximum expected returns but no impact on public health? I had been married at the time, I got married in 2012, so I'd been married for some number of years and I started thinking about what does my life mean? How am I as a partner to my wife if I'm spending my time just focused on making money and not actually doing something that can move the world forward? We had had our first baby at that point and I'm reflecting on just how impactful my parents were. I thought about what lessons I would be teaching my daughters. I got to the point where the combination of thinking across all those dimensions made me to question like, am I just doing the wrong thing, do I need to go in a different direction? And the answer was no. No, what I needed to do was take sort of the innate entrepreneurial energy that comes from my upbringing and my childhood. I needed to take the skill set that I was able to develop from this amazing apprenticeship that I had at Deerfield and be able to point those talents not just at the discovery side of being able to have invention, but be able to point that at the impact that's required to actually see these medicines have the impact they're intended to have. A lot of that was my inspiration for leaving Deerfield and wanting to go build Braidwell. I'm so fortunate because one of our dear friends, Brian Kreider, was also just as inspired as I was about where we found ourselves in the world and the type of impact that could happen but wasn't yet happening. Brian was the Chief Operating Officer for Bridgewater. I was co-running Deerfield and we decided to spend some really deep time trying to put our lessons learned and our insights together and try to design from a first principles perspective what would the operating system for human health look like and what could we do to be the driving force behind making that happen. How could we be the force that is not just okay with a once-in-a-decade hundred billion dollar GLP-1 revolution, but how do we be the force behind making sure that this once-in-a-lifetime trillion-dollar cost savings revolution happens? We want to be the force behind medicines having their moment where they could prevent diseases that are already preventable today. It really inspired us and after seeing it, we couldn't unsee it. Then we spent many more months thinking about how do we go do it, how would we build it, and that became Braidwell where I'm spending my time today and we're having the most fun that we've ever had. And if you could sum up the investment approach in its simplest form, how would you do it? I think I'd just bring you into what we call our morning meeting. So at Braidwell we get together the team every morning from whenever until we're done. And I want you to imagine you're in this room. You've seen it. So you're in this room, it's a big room, and we've got an amazingly talented team of scientists, biostatisticians, commercial experts, AI experts, investors, operating people, traders, and structured finance people. We have a pretty significant team and everybody on the team has some special superpower that they can contribute to us trying to figure out what companies have amazing technologies that we should be getting behind and backing and helping them to go as fast as possible. We want to have human impact that cuts at some of those big unlocks that would be the difference between having something and having it have its impact. Then, as we get to look across the ecosystem of everything that is moving forward today, where do we also see the major gaps where medicines should exist but they don't yet? To me, being in that room is the most fun part of the day and I think it very much epitomizes what our philosophy is. In order to be a great backer of innovation we essentially need to be able to answer three questions: are the innovations we're getting behind going to work or fail, are they ultimately going to be relevant from a market potential or not, and is there a way by which we could back this company and enable this company to move forward faster with capital that produces a return that's attractive enough for the investors? So we want to bring together the most talented minds at each of those different areas of domain expertise to give us an advantage that we can in a replicable manner deploy day after day after day to both be backing the companies that should be moving forward faster and making exist the companies that don't but should. It's an incredible room to be in. It makes me wonder like if everyone could design their own room to be in every day, it'd be a fun exercise. I know you're also a builder, we'll save that discussion for another time. Unfortunately we're out of time. I could literally—I mean I have pages of notes. We do this every time we talk. We set aside an hour and we should have set aside a day, but I'll go to my traditional closing question for this session and I'm sure we'll do this many times together over the years to come. What is the kindest thing that anyone's ever done for you? Oh, that's my wife. Look, I'm pretty darn hard at it every day, every minute of the day I can be hard at it and you know that drives me to different levels of stress and I could be so myopically deep down trying to figure something out that I kind of just lose track of everything else around me. My wife, Cass, has this amazing ability to just know when I'm at that, just on the precipice of that breaking point, and she could find some way to distract me, make me laugh, and kind of get me back to neutral. I think that's a big force and an advantage for me because I don't have to worry about going so hard. I've got a partner that's right there by my side that can help make sure that I could not just be going after the things I really care about doing, but having some fun along the way. A Fireside Chat With A Venture Capital Legend

Forbes(2.1M) ·46:55 ·1.2K views · 2026-04-21
Bill Gurley (Venture Capitalist)
To achieve elite career success, professionals must move beyond "passion" toward "fascination"—a deep, obsessive curiosity that fuels the repetitive learning necessary to master nuance and navigate disruptive shifts like AI.
TL;DR To achieve elite career success, professionals must move beyond "passion" toward "fascination"—a deep, obsessive curiosity that fuels the repetitive learning necessary to master nuance and navigate disruptive shifts like AI. Fascination vs. Passion: Gurley distinguishes...

TL;DR

To achieve elite career success, professionals must move beyond "passion" toward "fascination"—a deep, obsessive curiosity that fuels the repetitive learning necessary to master nuance and navigate disruptive shifts like AI.

The "Fascination" Framework for Career Longevity

  • Fascination vs. Passion: Gurley distinguishes "fascination" as an intellectual curiosity that makes work feel like play.
      • While passion can be a fleeting infatuation, fascination drives the "reps" (repetitive study) required to understand the nuance of a field.
      • The Nuance Edge: Innovation occurs at the edge of disruption; only those deeply obsessed with a topic will notice the subtle shifts (nuance) that create opportunity.
  • The "Social Media" Test: To find your fascination, determine if your field of study can compete with social media or Netflix for your attention. If you are naturally drawn to breakthroughs in your industry during your spare time, you are in the right field.
  • Pivoting with Purpose: Gurley emphasizes that careers are multi-step journeys. He pivoted from computer engineering to Wall Street to Venture Capital.
      • The 15-Year Rule: Founders and professionals should ask: "If I am still doing this 15 years from now, will I be happy?" If the answer is no, you are likely "climbing the wrong ladder."

Strategic Insights for Founders and VCs

  • Founder Archetypes: Beyond "Product-Market Fit," Gurley looks for specific human traits:
      • Unbridled Determinism: A relentless drive that cannot be stopped (a trait also prioritized by Jeff Bezos).
      • Sales Ability: A founder’s 10x ability to "close" investors, employees, and customers is a primary driver of success.
      • Unfair GTM Advantage: Successful startups often find a "go-to-market" advantage that doesn't rely on rote advertising or traditional sales forces.
  • The Defensibility of Software: Despite the "SaaS-pocalypse" narrative, software remains defensible when it serves as a "system of record" for deterministic data (e.g., financial ledgers) rather than just a workflow tool that AI can easily replicate.
  • The Danger of Over-Funding: Gurley warns against the "VC trap."
      • Equity Dilution: Founders often see their equity drop from 90% to 10% through successive rounds (A through D).
      • The Tito’s Model: He cites Bert "Tito" Beverage (Tito’s Vodka) as a model for success—he owns 100% of a company generating $400M in free cash flow because he used "sweat equity" instead of venture capital.

Network Effects and Peer Groups

  • The MrBeast "Hive Mind": Gurley highlights Jimmy Donaldson (MrBeast) as the ultimate example of leveraging a peer group.
      • Donaldson spent 16 hours a day on Skype for four years with three peers, analyzing YouTube.
      • 40,000-Hour Rule: By sharing insights independently learned, the group effectively achieved 40,000 hours of mastery in the time it took to do 10,000.
  • Value vs. Penetration: High-value network effects occur when the 10,000th customer provides significantly more value to the network than the 1,000th.
      • Businesses like OpenTable thrive on this "winner-take-most" trajectory, whereas "commodity" networks (like manual labor marketplaces) often plateau.

AI and the Future of Work

  • Inequality Gap: AI acts as an accelerator. The "learn-it-alls" who are already fascinated by their work will use AI to run even faster, potentially widening the gap between top performers and the rest of the workforce.
  • Personalization over Curation: In consumer tech, the next wave involves AI moving beyond mere categorization to making complex decisions on behalf of the individual, solving the "paradox of choice" in sectors like travel and real estate.
  • Healthcare Opportunity: Gurley acknowledges a massive shift toward AI in drug discovery (e.g., Parkinson's and Alzheimer's), noting that "technology for health" is likely the next trillion-dollar industry.
"Fascination involves work... when you're fascinated by something, you're endlessly curious about it and you just constantly want to study it and know more. Innovation is really about understanding nuance because it's the edge of disruption in your field." — Bill Gurley
"Ideas are a dime a dozen; the more you share, the more will come back to you. People get overly worried they have proprietary knowledge—sharing is a hive mind version of the 10,000-hour rule." — Bill Gurley
"I would warn more people about NOT taking venture capital... very quickly, and almost in a flash before you know it, your founder's equity goes from 90% to 10%. Find a way to use hustle and sweat equity." — Bill Gurley

Whoever the founder is represents the company, and I've seen people that have 10x sales ability relative to someone else and it really matters. Some way of getting customers that's not rote advertising or rote sales people. If you can figure that out... you know, there's this whole product-led growth meme. That stuff really matters. Cool. I really appreciate you. Good luck. Hi Bill, thanks for being here. My name is Thor Weber, I'm the founder of Skilldome; we do AI tutors. My question to you is around defensibility, and specifically: is there any defensibility left in software? I think it partially depends. If I had to bet on whether "SaaS-pocalypse" is overdone or not, I would say it's probably overdone a little bit. But I've always been a big believer in Warren Buffett's "be greedy when others are fearful and fearful when others are greedy." I do think the category matters. If your product was helping someone translate it from here to here, the AI can do that. If you're a system of record on finance, I think it makes no sense whatsoever that you would advise someone to code their own financial ledger. The auditor needs to be able to see this thing; it needs to be quite deterministic. And so I think the software that's safer is one where there's a need for deterministic data. Then you can use that data to reinforce whatever the AI model is. Other people are talking about this; I think people are kind of down on workflow and more optimistic about data centricity. But the truth of the matter is you have to know what AI can do in your field. It goes back to this curiosity thing because if you're not scared to death that you lack the knowledge of exactly what AI can do where you play every day, you might get run over. Well, fear... scared to death, but also to your point about curiosity and being fascinated, if you're excited about it, you're going to be the one disrupting because you've already figured out how to hack it, right? Most of the founders I know, that weekend Open Claude came out, they ran and bought a Mac mini and popped this thing up just because they didn't want to not know what everyone was talking about. It kind of requires that mentality. If you're curious, that happened innately. If you're telling yourself, "Oh, I should be doing this," you're a step behind. Hi, good morning Bill. My name is Anuroop Kumar, I'm the co-founder of Inthdeck Digital, it's a growth marketing firm, and also the co-host of the Culture x Commerce podcast. Big fan of your work. My question was on network effects. One of the first paid gigs I had was as a campus ambassador for Uber when they made its go-to-market in Tucson, Arizona. My question was around when you see founders that have had successful network effect motions—OpenTable, Uber—versus those you've seen have maybe not been able to execute. What were some of the key differences that you saw? I actually think that you can impact this a little bit. Imagine a graph where on the vertical axis you have value to the customer—and that could be either side of the marketplace—and on the horizontal axis you have penetration in the market. You want that line to go up. How does the 10,000th customer have way more value than the 1,000th customer? How can you make the value function for what you're doing dependent on the variable of how many people are in the system? I do think you can move that line around a little bit. In certain industries it's stronger form; some top out. When Amazon launched Mechanical Turk, a lot of people thought that would have a network effect, but I think if you aggregate 100 people that can do a commodity job, the line kind of levels out. If you had 100,000, it doesn't really change the quality of the work that much. So thinking about industries where that line keeps going up or the steepness of it is what you want to pay attention to. Hi Bill, my name is Kira and I'm a Salesforce software engineer at ANZ Bank in New Zealand. Well, there's a chapter in the book titled "Go Where the Action Is," so this would be the antithesis of that unless you like lamb. The answer is going to have to be in virtual communities. You want to be the most virtually connected person you can possibly be. Plus take advantage of the time difference; when they wake up, they always have a note from you. Hi, good morning Bill. I think it's a wonderful idea while you're trying to discover. There's an anecdote in the book about one of the guys that's part of the Acquired podcast; he had a thing called a side hustle. He'd show up at a job and ask his boss, "Do you mind if I do this on the side in my spare time?" and that launched Microsoft Garage when he was at Microsoft. That connected him to the entire venture community. He got an offer to go work at a venture firm which he thought he might like, but when he got there he said, "Can I launch a podcast on the side?" and they said "Sure," and that ended up being the thing he loves. So doing multiple things is a great way to AB test or experiment and AB test what you're fascinated with. I don't know how many Steve Jobs and Elon Musks there are in the world, so assuming you can be that, I would say, is a bit aggressive. I think if you want to really dent the world, you probably want to find a single lane at some point. Hello Mr. Gurley, my name is Tiffany Johnson and I'm an MD candidate from Stanford School of Medicine and the founder of The Med Bubble, which is a mental health platform for physicians and healthcare professionals. Part of the issue becomes a pipeline one of how you draw more people in. There was a point in my career where I think I was on 10 boards and 6 of them had female CEOs. I don't say that it was intentional; I was backing the best people I possibly could. This has been improving, but we should keep tilting harder against it. Hi Bill, I'm Sarah Romanco, I'm a VC. SUSECON 26 Opening Keynote Session with Dirk-Peter van Leeuwen | CEO, SUSE

SUSE(21.6K) ·27:21 ·153 views · 2026-04-21
Dirk-Peter van Leeuwen (CEO, SUSE)
SUSE is positioning itself as the primary alternative to proprietary vendor lock-in by offering a "resilience through choice" framework, headlined by the launch of the SUSE AI Factory with NVIDIA and a comprehensive portfolio expansion onto Oracle Cloud Infrastructure.
TL;DR SUSE is positioning itself as the primary alternative to proprietary vendor lock-in by offering a "resilience through choice" framework, headlined by the launch of the SUSE AI Factory with NVIDIA and a comprehensive portfolio expansion onto Oracle Cloud Infrastructure. ...

TL;DR

SUSE is positioning itself as the primary alternative to proprietary vendor lock-in by offering a "resilience through choice" framework, headlined by the launch of the SUSE AI Factory with NVIDIA and a comprehensive portfolio expansion onto Oracle Cloud Infrastructure.

Strategic Vision: Resilience through Choice

  • The "2026 Reality": CEO Dirk-Peter van Leeuwen defines the current landscape as "running on quicksand," characterized by fragmented supply chains, aggressive vendor pricing, and the rapid shift from Moore’s Law to "Wang’s Law" (accelerated AI-driven innovation).
  • Defining Resilience: In a modern enterprise context, resilience is not static; it is the ability to adapt and evolve without being held "hostage" by proprietary stacks.
  • Open Source as a Business Strategy: SUSE aims to bring open-source principles to business-critical environments, focusing on support and interoperability rather than just intellectual property.

The Five Pathways to Enterprise Resilience

  • Digital Sovereignty:
      • European Leadership: As a European-based company, SUSE offers a "Sovereignty Checker" and premium sovereign support to ensure data control and transparency.
      • Airbus Case Study: Mirko Reuter (SVP, Airbus Defence and Space) highlighted that open source is a "source of sovereignty" necessary for maintaining mission-critical systems for decades in "VUCA" (Volatile, Uncertain, Complex, Ambiguous) environments.
  • Hybrid Cloud Mastery:
      • Oracle Partnership: SUSE announced the delivery of its full portfolio on Oracle Cloud Infrastructure (OCI), enabling simple procurement and scaled workloads for AI and containerized apps.
  • Linux Modernization & Multi-Linux Support:
      • Legacy Freedom: SUSE now supports RHEL and CentOS estates, allowing customers to receive updates and support without migrating, effectively freezing costs while planning long-term transitions.
      • SLES 16: The latest OS version is designed specifically to handle the transition from VMs to containers and integrates agentic AI directly into the platform.
  • Enterprise AI (The AI Factory):
      • SUSE AI Factory with NVIDIA: A turnkey solution built on SUSE AI and NVIDIA AI Enterprise. It bridges local development to scalable production across edge and public cloud environments.
      • Private AI: Focuses on "private by design" models to ensure intelligence is not "leased" from proprietary black-box providers.
  • Industrial Edge & IIoT:
      • Losant Acquisition: Following the acquisition of Losant, SUSE is open-sourcing the technology to create the first full-stack open platform for the Industrial Internet of Things (IIoT).

Collaboration with NVIDIA

  • On-Premise Shift: John Fanelli (VP, NVIDIA) noted a significant trend of customers moving AI from Proof of Concept (POC) in the cloud to on-premise production to maintain data sovereignty.
  • Technical Integration: The partnership integrates SLES 16 and Rancher Prime with NVIDIA’s hardware-level operators and "NIM" (NVIDIA Inference Microservices).
  • Future Roadmap: NVIDIA teased the "Vera Rubin" platform, which combines new CPUs and GPUs with confidential computing capabilities to secure autonomous, agentic AI solutions.

Notable Quotes

"In 2026, your IT partner needs to set you free and not to put you in chains. SUSE can do that by bringing the open source principles back to life." — Dirk-Peter van Leeuwen, CEO, SUSE
"Open source is a source of sovereignty because it helps us build a supply system that we're basing our solutions on that we can fully rely on." — Mirko Reuter, SVP, Airbus Defence and Space
"If you rely on a proprietary black-box model, you have no resilience. You are leasing your intelligence from someone else." — Dirk-Peter van Leeuwen, CEO, SUSE
"It's almost like a circle of trust... our engineers know that they can trust one another in how they discuss the technology, and customers can trust that our joint solution will meet their needs." — John Fanelli, VP of Enterprise Software, NVIDIA

HGS's Global CEO Says AI Delivers ROI in 90 Days - Here's Exactly How | Front Page

AIM Network(297.0K) ·29:23 ·143 views · 2026-04-21
Venkatesh Korla (Global CEO, Hinduja Global Solutions)
Hinduja Global Solutions (HGS) is pivoting from a traditional BPO to a "Data and AI service provider," leveraging a 90-day "Return on Intelligent Experiences" (ROIX) framework to deliver 10-15% efficiency gains through agentic AI deployment and outcome-based pricing.
TL;DR Hinduja Global Solutions (HGS) is pivoting from a traditional BPO to a "Data and AI service provider," leveraging a 90-day "Return on Intelligent Experiences" (ROIX) framework to deliver 10-15% efficiency gains through agentic AI deployment and outcome-based pricing. ...

TL;DR

Hinduja Global Solutions (HGS) is pivoting from a traditional BPO to a "Data and AI service provider," leveraging a 90-day "Return on Intelligent Experiences" (ROIX) framework to deliver 10-15% efficiency gains through agentic AI deployment and outcome-based pricing.

The 90-Day ROI Framework (ROIX)

  • The ROIX Methodology: HGS uses a "forward deployment engineering team" to identify and transform top-tier processes within a 90-day window.
      • Phase 1 (Days 1-30): Identification of high-value processes and deployment of agentic AI using HGS's proprietary Agent X tooling.
      • Phase 2 (Days 31-90): Staff education, model fine-tuning, and performance monitoring.
  • Measurable Outcomes: The goal is a 10-15% improvement in efficiency, cost reduction, or revenue generation within the first three months.
  • Current Adoption: Approximately 30-40% of HGS’s current work is delivered via AI co-working with human team members.

Strategic Pivot: From BPO to AI-First Service Provider

  • The "Trifecta" Strategy: CEO Venkatesh Korla defines HGS's competitive edge as the intersection of process management pedigree, deep technology capability, and real-world AI application.
  • Rebranding Significance: The new "fingerprint" logo represents the collective digital and process history of the team, signaling a shift in behavior toward being an "intelligent experience" company.
  • Financial Impact:
      • Margin Growth: HGS has seen gross margin improvements of approximately 30% in the last quarter due to AI efficiencies.
      • Revenue Cannibalization: Korla acknowledges that while AI improves efficiency, it causes short-term revenue cannibalization on older, time-based contracts.
      • New Business: HGS closed 70+ new logos in the last six months following its strategic repositioning.

The Evolution of Pricing and Talent

  • Shift to Outcome-Based Pricing: HGS is moving away from "time and material" models toward value-based pricing.
      • Bottleneck: Korla notes that while businesses want outcome-based models, corporate procurement and contracting departments are not yet equipped to measure "value" over "hours."
  • The "AI Native" Talent Strategy:
      • Freshers: HGS is targeting "AI natives"—younger workers who intuitively use AI interfaces over traditional search engines.
      • The Seniority Gap: There is a long-term risk that if AI performs "junior-level" tasks, the industry may struggle to develop the next generation of senior leaders.
      • AI Illiteracy: A significant challenge remains in reskilling mid-level staff to move past simply consuming AI output and toward "wielding" the tool through iterative prompting.

Technology Stack and Industry Outlook

  • Preferred Tools: Korla is an avid user of Anthropic’s Claude (Opus, Chat, and Code), citing its ability to drive significant developer productivity over competitors.
  • Development of SLMs: HGS is building industry-specific and process-specific Small Language Models (SLMs), including both discriminative and predictive models.
  • The Human Element: Despite the push for automation, Korla maintains that "empathetic conversation" remains the one element of customer experience that AI will never fully replace.

Notable Quotes

"Applying AI to real-world processes and improving them is the magic sauce... Technology is going to get disrupted, coding is going to get disrupted, but applying it to business is where the magic happens." — Venkatesh Korla
"I personally believe people calling out 'AI revenue' individually is a misnomer. AI is like electricity; it is going to be embedded into everything that you do." — Venkatesh Korla
"A 21-year-old today is essentially the equivalent of an AI native who can wield the tool much faster than a 45-year-old. They don't even go to Google Search; they go to an AI chat interface by default." — Venkatesh Korla
"What is more dangerous: AI hype or AI underestimation? AI hype." — Venkatesh Korla

The intent being that we are able to go in, we deploy a forward deployment engineering team, which comes in, works with the company, and identifies the processes that they want to transform. Today if I can do 10 times as much work in the same amount of time it took me to do one time of that work, how will I get remunerated for all the knowledge that I have? The solution is outcome-based because it is based on the value I create for you as a customer. The 20 people are billed at $20 an hour, whereas the three people are billed at $60 an hour. It is still cheaper as a total cost of ownership to use the three people than the 20 people because you'll be able to produce more quickly and more effectively. So that same thing applies today in the world of AI. You want to price it based on outcome. The problem is: what are the metrics to measure outcomes? What are the metrics to measure value? That is what people are struggling with, and once we figure that piece out, we'll be able to do it. At HGS, when we take on a process in that first 90-day thing, we actually work with customers to define what is your success criteria and how would you measure it. Then we would do standard time and material or fixed fee type pricing. Ideally, we want to do outcome-based pricing because both the customer's objectives and our objectives are aligned. Howard Marks on Value Investing, AI in Finance & More – Wharton School Investor Series

Wharton School(191.0K) ·41:26 ·7.6K views · 2026-04-21
Howard Marks (Co-Chairman, Oaktree Capital)
Successful long-term investing relies on disciplined risk control and understanding that "it’s not what you buy, it’s what you pay.
TL;DR Successful long-term investing relies on disciplined risk control and understanding that "it’s not what you buy, it’s what you pay." Investors should focus on avoiding "losers" in credit markets while remaining wary of the psychological swings between optimism and...

TL;DR

Successful long-term investing relies on disciplined risk control and understanding that "it’s not what you buy, it’s what you pay." Investors should focus on avoiding "losers" in credit markets while remaining wary of the psychological swings between optimism and pessimism that drive market cycles.

Current Market Environment and Psychology

  • The Pendulum of Sentiment: Investor psychology often swings from "flawless to hopeless" with little middle ground.
      • Optimism as a Default: Investing is inherently optimistic; you give money away today in hopes of more later. This leads markets to ignore bad news until a "critical mass" of negativity forces a correction.
      • Cognitive Dissonance: Marks highlights the human tendency to reject information that contradicts existing beliefs, causing investors to hold onto optimistic outlooks even when warning signs appear.
  • The "Temperature" Today: Markets are currently in a period of retrenchment after a massive run-up (the S&P 500 rose ~87% between 2023 and 2025).
      • Valuation Concerns: The S&P 500 P/E ratio sits around 22x, significantly higher than the historic average of 16-17x.
      • Current Catalysts: Recent volatility is driven by "cockroaches" (emerging credit defaults), geopolitical tensions in Iran affecting energy, and the "AI shock" where new models from OpenAI/Anthropic threatened the long-term viability of software coding and related debt.

The Philosophy of "Fewer Losers"

  • The Tennis Analogy: Marks distinguishes between "Winner’s Games" and "Loser’s Games" using professional vs. amateur tennis.
      • Pro Tennis (More Winners): Success comes from hitting the most "winners"—unreachable shots. This applies to high-upside fields like Venture Capital.
      • Amateur Tennis (Fewer Losers): Success comes from hitting the fewest "losers"—unforced errors. The player who simply keeps the ball in play longest wins.
  • Credit vs. Equity Strategy:
      • In Credit Investing, you don't need "winners" because your upside is capped at the interest rate. You win by ensuring no one defaults.
      • Oaktree’s philosophy is built on risk control and consistency rather than swinging for the fences.

Redefining Value and Growth

  • The Artificial Bifurcation: The traditional divide between "Value" (cheap, slow-growing) and "Growth" (expensive, fast-growing) is often counterproductive.
      • Value in Growth: A fast-growing tech company can still be a "value" investment if the price paid is appropriate for its future earnings.
      • Price vs. Quality: Marks emphasizes that "good investing doesn't come from buying good things; it comes from buying things well." Even the best company (like the "Nifty Fifty" in the 1970s) can be a terrible investment if the entry price is too high.
  • The Lessons of Failure: Marks notes that success teaches nothing; real investment wisdom is garnered from enduring market crashes and seeing once-revered assets lose 95% of their value.

Strategic Discipline: Buying, Selling, and Timing

  • The Fallacy of "The Bottom": Marks argues that "waiting for the bottom" is a fool’s errand because the bottom is only identifiable the day after it has passed.
      • Focus on Cheapness: Investors should buy when assets are objectively cheap relative to their intrinsic value, regardless of whether they might go lower in the short term.
  • Selling as "Unbuying": To avoid emotional exits, investors should view selling as a decision to "unbuy" an asset.
      • The Mental Framework: Ask, "If I didn't own this today, would I buy it at this price?" If the answer is no, a sale is justified.
  • The Danger of Selling Early: Using Amazon as an example (which dropped from $90 to $6 and eventually rose to $3,300), Marks warns against selling just because an asset is "up." Great ideas are rare; the biggest mistake is "getting off the train" too soon.
"The bottom is the day before it starts going up. And if that's true, then by definition you never know when you're at the bottom because you can only tell the next day." — Howard Marks
"It's not what you buy, it's what you pay that counts. Good investing doesn't come from buying good things, it comes from buying things well." — Howard Marks
"When you see one cockroach, there are probably more." — Howard Marks (quoting Jamie Dimon)
"In the minds of investors, things go from flawless to hopeless. Psychology swings to the extreme. First they like them too much, then they hate them too much." — Howard Marks

One grounded in discipline, patience, and a deep respect for market cycles. Since Oaktree's founding in 1995, Howard has served as both steward and communicator of these core principles, guiding investment strategy while translating complex market dynamics into ideas that resonate far beyond the firm's walls. He is perhaps best known for many of his memos, widely read, widely shared, and often treated as required readings across the investment community. In them, he distills decades of experience into reflections that are at once practical and philosophical, offering a rare combination of accessibility and depth. These ideas are further explored in his books, including *The Most Important Thing* and *Mastering the Market Cycle*, where he examines how investors can better understand and navigate the inevitable swings in financial markets. Beyond investing, Howard has remained deeply committed to education and intellectual exchange. Let's take that and turn it to your philosophy of investing and talk a bit about growth and value cycles. In part because this was sort of echoed in the conversation we had before you came onto the dais today. There have been a lot of statements about how value investing has suffered, some authors say that it's dead with respect to whatever that means. We hear statements about the Mag 7 at one point having been value stocks even though they've been growing. At one point as a result of your conversations with your son Andrew during the pandemic, you mentioned that your philosophy of growth may have changed a little bit. Where does it stand now, in part because you can look at the shift from once again now what was intangible to tangible value, shareholder common equity is suddenly back in. Has it continued to evolve or do you think this is really just an extension of what we all always have seen with value growth? Well, hopefully it keeps evolving because that means you keep thinking. But prior to the 60s, people investing in the stock market were called investors. There were no different kinds of stock market investors. Then, around the beginning of the 60s, Wall Street, I remember seeing a brochure in my dad's apartment in around '62, talking about something called growth stocks. And the concept of growth stocks was invented, and these were companies that looked expensive on the basis of their current assets or earnings, but had brilliant futures. And they were either in tech or biotech or technological advancement, or they were great consumer companies. So you had Xerox, IBM, Kodak, Polaroid, Hewlett-Packard, Perkin-Elmer, Texas Instruments, Merck, Lilly, etc., or you had Coca-Cola for example, Sears Roebuck, etc. A growth company... all companies grow, growth companies grow at above-average rates of growth. And so the growth stocks started to attract attention, people started to specialize in the growth stocks, most of the money center banks, where I started to work in '69, Citibank for example, gravitated toward something called the Nifty Fifty, which were the epitome of the growth stocks. So as humans do, to categorize themselves, they want to put labels on. So the people who did not invest in growth stocks called themselves value. And value came to be a firm bifurcation. These are growth stocks, they grow faster than the average company. These are value stocks, they're cheaper than the average company. So that became the dichotomy. And growth stock investing came to be identified with kind of prosaic companies which are not attractive on the face because of their potential, they're mundane or slow growing or traditional, but because they're not sexy, they sell at low prices and they're better value. And so some people buy the growth stocks, some people buy the value stocks, but with this firm divide. And Andrew's... so Andrew and his family moved in with us during the pandemic and we got to exchange a lot of ideas, a lot of them were on this subject and I crystallized them in a memo I wrote in January of '21 called "Something of Value" because we were talking about value investing and because the opportunity for three generations to live together was of great value. And you know, the bottom line was it shouldn't be a bifurcation, it shouldn't be either-or. But you can make an intelligent investment in a company with a high rate of growth or in a low rate of growth, and you should pay a price which is appropriate for the rate of growth. But just because a company is fast growing doesn't mean it can't be a good value investment. And you gotta get away from these hard exclusionary definitions. And I think that was right. I think when you interviewed Steve Schwarzman here, there was an echo of value that led across the conversation in the sense that one of the most important things out of many is the price at which you buy the asset. Yes, well, you know, so I mentioned that the banks were involved with the Nifty Fifty. So I got out of grad school in '69—I had to spend a little time in the army reserves to fulfill my obligation—and then I started to work in Citibank in late September of '69. And the bank, as I said, bought these Nifty Fifty stocks. And if you got there and bought the stocks the day I started work, in late '69, and if you held them tenaciously because of resolve, because of dedication, because of intellectual commitment, if you held them for five years, guess what happened? You lost about 95% of your money. Because number one, bad things did happen to half the companies, which were overestimated fundamentally, and number two, they were all overpriced. So if you buy something which is where the merits are overestimated and the merits are overpriced, you're probably going to have a bad experience. And the Nifty Fifty investor was decimated. And so... but you hopefully will learn lessons in your career, and the greatest thing is if you can learn them early. And you learn nothing from success, you learn all your lessons from failure. And I learned a good lesson right away in my first decade in the business, which is it's not what you buy, it's what you pay that counts. Good investing doesn't come from buying good things, it comes from buying things well. And you have to understand that the difference is more than grammatical. And there is no asset which is so good that it can't become overpriced and dangerous. And there are very few assets which are so bad that if they get cheap enough, they can't be a good buy. So those were the lessons that I learned in my first decade and they have stood me in very good stead ever since. And so it's all a matter of appraising the merits objectively and seeing if you can buy them at an attractive price. Every trade is two trades. So there's the question of buying and there's the question of selling. How do you map your logic to discipline in selling? The good news about buying a bond of a firm that's in distress is that you have finite maturity. But that's not true for many investments that have the scintillating character that you're describing today. I wrote a memo around ten years ago, it was probably around 2017, called "Selling Out." And I said in there, why do people sell? You know, you read a hundred articles and books and analyses on whether to buy something, there are very few on whether to sell it. And I said in the memo, why do people sell? And half-facetiously, I said they sell for two reasons: because it went up and because it went down. If it goes up, they sell it because they're afraid that it's going to stop going up and maybe go down, in which case their profits will evaporate. And if it goes down, they sell it because it scares them and makes them think it might go down more. Now if you think about it logically, it can't possibly be right to sell because it went up and to sell because it went down. So that just shows the illogic of the situation. So when should you sell? And I think the answer is you should look at the decision to sell as the decision to unbuy. And I think that clarifies it. So what you do is you make believe you don't have it, you analyze it, and you say, "Should I buy it today?" And if the answer is you shouldn't buy it today, then maybe you should sell it. Now there's a whole category of things that you shouldn't either buy or sell, and those are probably things you should hold. And that's a valid thing and you can't be... I think it's pretending too much cleverness to say that everything is either a buy or a sell. Some things are just holds. They're kind of in the middle, they're price fair, and if they have the potential to keep improving in terms of the underlying company, it might be fine to own them. They're not bargains, you shouldn't fall over yourself to buy them, but on the other hand, they're not overpriced or negatively presented and you should sell them. But so I think that if you think about the decision to sell as the decision to unbuy, I think it's more helpful. And you know, there are all these sayings and I've been... I'm a very conservative person and I was raised by parents who were adults during the Depression. I'll bet none of you can say that. But my parents were adults during the Depression. They were born before 1910. And so what did I hear every day? "Don't put all your eggs in one basket," "Save for a rainy day," all these cautionary mottos. Your generation didn't hear these, or your parents' generation didn't hear these because their parents weren't alive during the Depression. And what you hear as a kid shapes your personality and your behavior. But so because I'm biased toward caution, I came up with, "Well, you can always take a little off the table." You know, you can reduce your risk somewhat, you can bank your cost and let your profits ride. Or one of my stupid sayings was, "If you sell half, you can't be all wrong." And all of these things are too prone toward selling. If I'd been more of an optimist, I would have made more money. Think about... and I think in this memo on selling out, I told the story of Amazon. And as I recall, Amazon was 90 in '99, on the tech bubble, and then when the bubble burst in 2000 or 2001, it was 6. So it went from 90 to 6, it was down 93%. So what if you were smart enough to buy it at 6? Would you have held it at 12? Or would you have said, "Well, I've doubled my money, I'm going to take out my cost and let my profits ride." And let's say you held it at 12, you're tough. What about when it got to 60? And you've made ten times your money, would you sell it? Most people would. What about when it got to 600? And you've made a hundred times your money, would you sell half, would you sell three quarters, would you sell 90%? And at the time I wrote it, as I recall, Amazon was 3300. So if you sold it at 600 when it was up a hundred times, you left basically 85% of the money on the table. So it should not be your natural inclination to sell because things are up. And Buffett says he made all his money on twelve ideas. Guy invested for 70 years, he says he made all his money on twelve ideas. Charlie Munger, who was a friend of mine, Warren's partner, used to say he made all his money on four ideas. He didn't have as many good ideas as Warren. So if you realize how scarce they are, and that you're not going to find a lot of them, then the big mistake is getting off too soon. So I think that that is something that has to be redressed. So are we in a world where we should be stretching, or are we in a world where we should be thinking about making money by not losing it? I'll just take a minute. So one of the influential things I read was 1974, there's a guy named Charlie Ellis, who is a well-known kind of thinker and advisor to investing companies. And Charlie wrote an article called "Winning the Loser's Game." I don't know if you've ever heard of TRW. It was one of the first credit rating agencies for individuals like Experian and that kind of thing. And there was a guy named Si Ramo, who was the R in TRW, and he wrote a book... I'm a tennis player and he wrote a book, *Extraordinary Tennis for Ordinary Tennis Players*. And he made an interesting observation. He said that in professional tennis, in championship tennis, the person who wins the match is the person who hits the most winners. You have to go for winners. If you hit a ball back gently, your opponent will hit a winner and put it away. You'll lose every point. So you have to go for winners, but of course you have to have the skill to execute. And the winner of the championship tennis match will be the person who hit the most winners. But at the amateur or club level, where I play, the winner of the match is often the person who hits the fewest losers. Because the club tennis player doesn't have the ability to hit a lot of winners, and in fact, many don't have the ability to keep the ball in play. So I believe that if I can just hit it back over the net 20 times, my opponent can't and I'll win the point. I don't have to hit a winner, I just don't have to hit any losers. So Charlie Ellis took this idea to investing. Should you go for winners or should you try to avoid hitting losers? And that appealed to me and given my chicken nature, I said I can win by not hitting losers. And so when we... now Oaktree, my company, invests primarily in credit. In credit, you make a bunch of loans. If you can make a bunch of loans without having any that default, you'll get the return you were after. So that's a good formula in credit, or in fixed income, or in debt, or bonds, whatever you want to call it. It's not a good formula in venture capital, or even in equities, or maybe in real estate. You gotta have some winners. But in credit, avoiding the losers is good enough. And that appealed to my orientation. So when we set up Oaktree, we wrote down the investment philosophy: number one is risk control, number two is consistency. It's all about avoiding the losers. But it's a choice that everybody should make based on their skill level and their ambition. If the club player tries to hit a bunch of winners, it's going to be a disaster. It should be based on your skill level and your ambition. If you aspire to be a championship player, you should learn how to hit winners and then try to do it. If you don't learn how to hit winners and try to do it, you'll never become a champion. Of course, most people can't. So you have to pick your course, but it has to be the right course for you, not for me, not for you, not for your parents, but for you. And a lot of life is figuring out what you're about and then pursuing life accordingly. So I'm sorry I have to stop because this is fun, but thank you for having me here. Howard, thank you so much for the discussion. Thank you so much.

Confirmation Hearing: Federal Reserve Chair Nominee Kevin Warsh Testifies

C-SPAN(1.8M) ·150:35 ·17.9K views · 2026-04-21
Kevin Warsh (Federal Reserve Chair Nominee)
Kevin Warsh defended his nomination to lead the Federal Reserve, pledging strict independence from political pressure while proposing a "regime change" in Fed policy that prioritizes the interest rate tool over the balance sheet and leverages productivity gains from AI to combat inflation.
TL;DR Kevin Warsh defended his nomination to lead the Federal Reserve, pledging strict independence from political pressure while proposing a "regime change" in Fed policy that prioritizes the interest rate tool over the balance sheet and leverages productivity gains from AI to...

TL;DR

Kevin Warsh defended his nomination to lead the Federal Reserve, pledging strict independence from political pressure while proposing a "regime change" in Fed policy that prioritizes the interest rate tool over the balance sheet and leverages productivity gains from AI to combat inflation.

Fed Independence and "Mission Creep"

  • Commitment to Independence: Warsh asserted that monetary policy independence is "essential" and must be protected by the Fed itself. He argued that independence is not threatened by elected officials stating their views, but by the Fed failing to deliver on its mandate.
  • Staying in the Lane: Warsh and Republican senators, including Chairman Tim Scott (R-SC) and Sen. Bill Hagerty (R-TN), criticized the Fed’s recent "mission creep" into political issues like climate change and social engineering.
      • Policy Neutrality: Warsh vowed to return the institution to its "knitting," focusing strictly on price stability and maximum employment.
  • Political Pressure: Democrats, led by Ranking Member Elizabeth Warren (D-MA) and Sen. Gallego, questioned Warsh’s ability to resist President Trump.
      • Rate Cut Allegations: Sen. Gallego cited reports that Trump "pressed" Warsh on rate cuts during his interview. Warsh testified under oath that the President never asked for a commitment to any specific interest rate decision.
      • "Sock Puppet" Accusation: Sen. Warren characterized Warsh as a "sock puppet" for the President, claiming his shifting views on interest rates align more with political convenience than economic data.

Proposed Monetary Policy "Regime Change"

  • Tool Prioritization: Warsh expressed a strong preference for using the interest rate tool over the balance sheet tool (Quantitative Easing).
      • Equity Concerns: He argued that a large balance sheet disproportionately benefits those with financial assets, whereas interest rate changes "get in the cracks" and affect the broader economy more fairly.
  • Communication Reform: Warsh proposed moving away from "forward guidance" and "the dots" (forecasts). He argued that pre-committing to rate paths leads to "groupthink" and prevents the Fed from correcting errors quickly when data changes.
  • The AI Productivity "Bet": Warsh identified Artificial Intelligence as the most disruptive economic force of the modern era.
      • Disinflationary Force: He suggested that AI-driven productivity gains could allow for faster economic growth and lower interest rates without triggering inflation.
      • Scientific Skepticism: Sens. John Kennedy (R-LA) and Chris Van Hollen (D-MD) questioned whether this "productivity boom" is speculative hype being used to justify premature rate cuts.

Ethics, Divestiture, and Past Record

  • Asset Divestiture: Warsh confirmed he has reached an agreement with the Office of Government Ethics (OGE) to divest "virtually all" of his financial assets—estimated at over $100 million—within 90 days of confirmation.
      • Ethics Disputes: Sen. Jack Reed (D-RI) and Sen. Warren pushed Warsh to disclose the specific contents of his private fund holdings (e.g., Duquesne/Juggernaut) and to divest *before* taking the oath of office to avoid conflicts of interest.
  • 2008 Financial Crisis Record: Democratic senators challenged Warsh’s performance during his previous tenure as a Fed Governor (2006–2011).
      • Subprime Warnings: Sen. Catherine Cortez Masto (D-NV) accused Warsh of ignoring warnings from housing advocates about predatory lending prior to the crash.
      • Bailouts: Sen. Warren criticized him for facilitating "multi-billion dollar bailouts" for Wall Street while allegedly doing little for families facing foreclosure.

Economic Outlook and the "Dual Mandate"

  • Affordability Crisis: Warsh acknowledged that while the rate of inflation has slowed, the cumulative 25–35% price increase since 2020 remains a "legacy of past policy errors" that continues to squeeze American families.
  • Defining Price Stability: Warsh offered an "old-fashioned" definition of price stability: a state where "the change in prices is such that no one is talking about it."
  • Maximum Employment: Under questioning from Sen. Raphael Warnock (D-GA), Warsh reaffirmed that the employment mandate is equal to the price stability mandate, though he noted the Fed must remain nimble as technology shifts the labor market structure.

***

Notable Quotes

"Monetary policy independence is essential... Fed independence is up to the Fed. That has three implications: Congress is tasked with the mission to ensure price stability; Fed independence is at its peak in the conduct of monetary policy; and the Fed must stay in its lane." — Kevin Warsh
"The President never once asked me to commit to any particular interest rate decision period, nor would I ever agree to do so if he had. But he never did." — Kevin Warsh
"The Federal Reserve should not be ruled by pointy-headed economists pouring over outdated models and reams of market data. It needs a leader who understands that the Fed’s mandate... serves a higher purpose: the preservation of the American dream." — Sen. Dave McCormick
"Having a sock puppet in charge of the Fed would give the President access to the Fed’s powerful authorities to enrich himself, his family, and his Wall Street buddies... A Fed under Donald Trump’s control creates more opportunities for Trump’s corruption." — Sen. Elizabeth Warren

The monetary policy choices made at the Federal Reserve can affect the Americans' ability to buy groceries, whether or not they can afford a home, and how far their paycheck goes, especially at the end of the month. And when the Fed is successful in fulfilling its dual mandate, affordability improves, with Americans taking home more money and paying less for everyday necessities. Unfortunately, in recent years, the Fed has faced real challenges. There have been questions about its decisions, its focus, and whether it has engaged in issues far outside its jurisdiction. For example, under the Biden administration, the Fed pushed climate-focused initiatives, only to reverse that climate agenda as soon as President Trump took office. The Fed has an interest rate tool and a balance sheet tool. My view is the interest rate tool gets in the cracks; it's fairer. The balance sheet tool disproportionately helps those with financial assets. The interest rate tool hits the entire economy. I think if the Fed were to wait until it gets into a meeting before making a decision, that incremental deliberation can keep the central bank from compounding its errors. Yeah, and core PCE inflation has been running at about 3%—a full point above that Fed target, correct? Yes. And so some Federal Reserve officials have said that this excess is due to tariffs. Do you agree with that? Senator, I don't. And if I can make two points, please. Much as we discussed in your office, I think the data that's being used to judge inflation is quite imperfect data. Among the projects that the economics profession and, if I'm confirmed as Chairman of the Fed, the Fed needs to do is to try to use our new understanding and new data sources to see what's really the inflation rate in the economy. We used to use core PCE measures so we'd exclude food and energy because it was sort of a rough swag as to what was going on. We don't have to use a rough swag anymore. What I'm most interested in is what's the underlying inflation rate—not what's the one-time change in prices because of a change in geopolitics or a change in beef, but what's the underlying generalized change in prices in the economy. My broad sense is that these inflation risks and the inflation damage of the last several years is improving somewhat. It has improved somewhat in the last year. The measures I prefer are looking at things that are called trimmed averages where we take out all of the tail risks, all of the one-off items, and we ask ourselves whether the generalized change in prices is having second-order effects on the economy. Again, they're not where they should be, but I think the trend is quite favorable. And these trend averages in the data that you're looking at now—it's not traditionally the norm that economists would use in this position, is that correct? Some in the economics profession are increasingly looking to these median-type measures. But among the projects I would hope to undertake as one of the first reforms at the Fed is a data project where we would go off and evaluate with the public sector and the private sector, including the Bureau of Labor Statistics, a survey of a billion prices. What I'm really most interested in, Senator, is what's the change of that 500 millionth and one price, because that's inflation. That's a change in the generalized level. In a market economy prices change all the time, and I don't want to be confused by that. I want to know what inflation really is, and I still think there's some work to do. I think that compounded the financial crisis. Second, I think subprime mortgages then—subprime assets then—were indicative of prices of almost every financial asset that were mispriced. What I suggested then and what I believe now is that subprime mortgages were just indicative of a set of prices that were incorrect and they all repriced. And what the Fed has done is eroded its credibility as what it should be, which is an apolitical and independent central bank. In bank regulation and supervision, the Fed's focused on non-material and often politically charged areas; I'm thinking about climate change policy here. Elsewhere in the Federal Reserve system we've seen regional reserve banks used as platforms for promoting very divisive partisan policies—there I'm thinking about DEI, climate policy, even racial reparations. The Minneapolis Fed even went so far as to publicly lobby for an amendment to a state constitution on education policy. How far beyond the remit can they get? In the 2020 monetary policy review, the Fed even sought to redefine its legislative mandate of maximum employment as a "broad-based and inclusive goal." I mean, that's tacitly understood to mean that the Fed should accept higher inflation to privilege select groups of Americans. It subsidized government financing and it encouraged, frankly, what I view as very reckless federal spending. In each of these examples we see a clear pattern: the Fed has strayed beyond its core mandate to encroach upon policy decisions that ought to be left to those of us that are democratically accountable. Welcome, Mr. Warsh, and congratulations on your nomination, and thank you for appearing before this committee and taking both the positive questions and some of the hostility. I apologize for my colleague's behavior when it strays outside of decorum. My first question is about the balance sheet of the Fed. What is your view about the size of the balance sheet currently? Thank you, Senator. As we talked about in your office, the Fed balance sheet has played a particularly, I think, unhelpful role in helping the Fed achieve its dual mandate. The Senator had just mentioned what she described as the increase for financial assets relative to real assets—well, part of that is the Federal Reserve. The Fed is not blameless in that. As the Fed's balance sheet has grown its imprimatur on the economy, those with financial assets have benefited from it. The reason why I prefer monetary policy to use interest rates as the dominant force is interest rates affect a far broader cross-section of the economy. Interest rates get in the cracks. If we were to cut rates, then a broader number of people will benefit from it versus quantitative easing, which tends to move through financial assets first. Half of our fellow Americans don't own any financial assets, so they're wondering what's in it for them. Broadly speaking, Senator, on the balance sheet: I was involved in 2008 and '09 in response to the global financial crisis that Senator Warren referenced, and we used extraordinary measures and created a bigger balance sheet. But we agreed to do that only in the times of emergency—only when interest rates were pinned to zero. The big balance sheet has become an ordinary recurring force and I think has been quite unhelpful, and is part of the reason why the Fed is in the business of politics. Slowly and deliberately, I believe we need a smaller central bank balance sheet. It took us 18 years to create this big balance sheet that's done quite a bit of harm, it strikes me, to the Fed's credibility. Working with the Treasury Secretary, we're going to have to find a way in which we can take the balance sheet and make it smaller. Because a large balance sheet where the Fed owns more outstanding debt than many parts of the financial markets... that's fiscal policy in disguise. Fed needs to get out of the fiscal business, focus on the monetary business, so the Fed can deliver on the remit you gave us. Thanks for that response, because some of us don't appreciate the connection between the size of the Fed's balance sheet and interest rates. With regard to interest rates, can you tell us a little bit about your conversation with President Trump about interest rates? Yeah, I don't want to put words in the President's mouth, but I think he has said loudly and clearly his view on interest rates—I don't think I'm breaking any news here. But I can tell you what I said. And what I said to the President is what I've frankly been writing about for more than a decade: interest rates are the much better way to be using monetary policy than buying bonds and mortgages, some of which are issued from the United States Treasury Department. That is a confusion of roles that leads to a set of mission creep. So interest rates are the dominant tool, and interest rates, as I mentioned, get in the cracks. My broad view on the conduct of policy from here is that too many Fed officials past and present opine in advance about where they think interest rates should be next meeting, next quarter, next year. I think that's quite unhelpful. As we've heard today from many of your colleagues, we don't know the state of AI. We can't be certain about the state of geopolitics. The economy is going to change. You need central bankers that are humble, that are nimble, that are open-minded, that can react when we have good data when events strike us. My broad view is that interest rates need to be forward-looking, need to be based on better data, and we should have a big robust deliberation inside the FOMC. I tend to favor messier meetings than some, where people don't show up with rehearsed scripts but we can have a good family fight. If the central bank has that good family fight, I think that they're going to make better decisions. And if they happen to make mistakes, they'll correct them sooner. So I'm not one for pre-deciding what interest rates should be. I never said to the President where I think rates should be. I never committed to any such thing, and I wouldn't have even thought about doing so. You have about 20 seconds to answer that. 20 seconds, Senator? "A lot" is the short answer. The 15-second answer beyond that is the pace of change in these technologies is accelerating. AI, which I think of really as American ingenuity, gives America a huge head start relative to our competitors around the world, but it's not without real risks and real challenges. A forward-looking, reform-oriented central bank needs to be on the front end of it. Great, thank you very much. I look forward to your leadership. Senator Gallego? Thank you, Mr. Chair. How are you? Introducing ChatGPT Images 2.0

OpenAI(1.9M) ·21:53 ·186.3K views · 2026-04-21
Sam Altman (CEO, OpenAI) Gabriel Goh (OpenAI) Kenji Hata (OpenAI)
OpenAI has launched ChatGPT Images 2.0 (Imagen 2.0), a major architectural leap that shifts from simple image generation to "visual reasoning," enabling perfect multilingual text rendering, multi-page document creation, and a "Thinking Mode" for complex, research-based visuals.
TL;DR OpenAI has launched ChatGPT Images 2.0 (Imagen 2.0), a major architectural leap that shifts from simple image generation to "visual reasoning," enabling perfect multilingual text rendering, multi-page document creation, and a "Thinking Mode" for complex, research-based...

TL;DR

OpenAI has launched ChatGPT Images 2.0 (Imagen 2.0), a major architectural leap that shifts from simple image generation to "visual reasoning," enabling perfect multilingual text rendering, multi-page document creation, and a "Thinking Mode" for complex, research-based visuals.

Next-Generation Visual Intelligence: "Thinking" vs. "Instant"

The model introduces a fundamental shift in how AI processes visual requests, moving beyond static generation to active deliberation and research.

  • Two Operational Modes:
      • Instant Mode: High-speed, high-quality visual intelligence available to all users for daily tasks.
      • Thinking Mode: Exclusive to paid users; the model "deliberates" before generating, allowing it to perform web searches, synthesize data, and plan complex layouts before final output.
  • Web Integration: The model can search the internet to incorporate real-time information, such as social media reactions or factual data, directly into generated infographics or posters.
  • Functional Problem Solving: It can generate images that solve math problems with proofs or create complex renovation plans based on specific spatial constraints.

Advanced Text Rendering and Multilingual Support

One of the most significant technical hurdles overcome in version 2.0 is the precise rendering of text and complex typography across various languages.

  • Typographic Precision: The model has virtually eliminated typos, capable of generating entire paragraphs of legible text and structured magazine layouts.
  • Global Language Capabilities:
      • Complex Scripts: Significant improvements in rendering Asian and Indic languages (Hindi, Chinese, Japanese, Korean, Telugu, etc.) which traditionally struggled due to high character counts.
      • Cultural Context: The model understands localized design aesthetics, such as creating a Japanese bakery poster with correct Kanji and Hiragana.
  • Macro-Detail: Demonstrated extreme precision by rendering legible text ("GPT Image 2") onto a single grain of rice within a massive pile.

Production-Ready Creative Tools

The update focuses on making the AI a tool for "inventing and building" rather than just artistic curiosity, offering features standard in professional photography and design.

  • Photorealism & "Naturalness": Includes a "photorealistic" trigger that replicates natural imperfections, graininess, and authentic lighting found in "shot on iPhone" or disposable camera photography.
  • Aspect Ratio Flexibility: Supports wide ranges from 1x3 (tall) to 3x1 (wide), including 360-degree seamless panoramas that can be used in VR/panorama viewers.
  • Resolution: Capabilities extend up to 4K resolution via API, providing extraordinary micro-details suitable for professional print and digital media.

Multi-Page Coherence and Iterative Design

ChatGPT Images 2.0 is designed to maintain character and style consistency across multiple frames, enabling sequential storytelling and complex design sheets.

  • Sequential Content: Users can generate entire manga comics or magazines from a single prompt while maintaining "recurring characters" and evolving storylines across multiple pages.
  • Interactive Design Sheets:
      • Fashion/Retail: The model can act as a stylist, taking a user's photo and generating a "fashion sheet" with different outfits, alternative views, and detailed callouts for specific items.
      • Brand Iteration: Users can upload a poster and ask the model to extract and generate 16-20 different logo variations based on that specific brand language.

Notable Quotes

"If we think of DALL-E as cave drawings, and Imagen 1 as ancient art, then Imagen 2.0 is the Renaissance. Imagen 2.0 is the smartest image generation model ever built."

— Sam Altman, CEO

"This model is producing images of a certain quality that... look just normal. After you look at these enough, you go back and look at previous images and you see all the mistakes... that you didn’t even notice before."

— Gabriel Goh, Research Lead

"This new model is no more like an AI image generator that you just give a prompt and it returns an image. It's more like an AI that you interactively talk to."

— Kiwhan Song, Researcher

"Image generation with thinking allows you to do really complex things such as web search, synthesize answers, and put a QR code all in one image."

— Kenji Hata, Researcher
